RATING: 7.5
My first hex-n-chit wargame.  I must say, I really like it.  Very strategic and tactical, with a good dose of lucky/unlucky events.  Feels very cinematic, too.  Every game creates a memorable narrative and it feels almost like I'm watching a movie, only I'm an active participant in it.

The card-driven aspect is new to me, but I really like it; it seems to be a very innovative and cool way to take care of die rolls, random events, the confusion that happens in the midst of battle, and the advancement of time, all together.

I even have a great time playing this solo, just imagining in my head how the battle is playing out.

Seems to take me a long time to do much in the game, though.

RATING: 8
GMT games are like crack to me. I want them and I want them to make me feel good about myself, but often they put me in a daze and ruin my life. I think it has something to do with the density of the rulebooks and the sneaking suspicion that I'm playing wrong in some way. However, there is still something about them that keeps me coming back. Combat Commander is a big reason why.

At first glance the game may look stodgy or square or for old people with no taste in the razzmatazz of modern board games. It looks like it should come with a bag of Werther's Originals. However, upon playing the game and seeing the excitement of opportunity fire, random sniping, triggered events, white-knuckle rally rolls, and unexpected swings of fortune, it reveals its true form - that of an unpredictable, yet satisfyingly strategic game of tactical prowess. 

It calls to me from the shelf, wanting me to match wits against a worthy opponent and see just what way the twelve scenarios (or countless generated scenarios) may go. It's just a brilliant game that is far more simple than at first glance and far more sweet for it. Just like a Werther's Original hard candy.

Fantastic stuff, endlessly engaging and variable. A truly great game.

ALSO WITH: Battle Packs 1, 2, 3, 5, 7.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
This is, quite simply, the most overrated wargame ever. I haven't played every tactical system, but I have played a lot of them: Squad Leader, Advanced Squad Leader, Up Front, Band of Brothers, Conflict of Heroes, Tide of Iron, Memoir 44, Frontline: D-Day, and Advanced Tobruk System. They all have one thing in common: they are better than Combat Commander.
So why is this game a dog? Well, first of all, we are on the battlefield. The enemy is closing in on our position.
Sir, what do we do?
Nothing.
Why? They'll kill us.
Because we don't have a fire card!
[ImageID=1707042original]
Sir, we found a stash of ammunition!
Good, fire at the sun!
The sun?
Do you have a problem hearing soldier?
No, sir.
Pew! Pew! Pew! Pew! Pew! Pew!
Sir, the sun is setting quickly!
Exactly! The sun is afraid is bullets. It is night time now. We have secured the area.
Sir, won't they attack us at night?
There are no night attacks.
Why not?
It's in the rules.
Oh, you are right. Clearly written rules that explain how to play this moronic "simulation."
Even funnier is listening to fans of this game try to rationalize its absurdities. If you don't have a card, you are performing "ineffective" fire, but a card represents "effective" fire...unless you miss.  Then it's ineffective.
And firing at the sun? I heard that the space-time continuum changes. On Turn 1, 1 hex may represent 100 yards and at the end of the game, it represents 200 miles.  However, the range of your weapons do not change. Go figure.
This game SUPPOSEDLY marries the tactics of Squad Leader with the chaos of Up Front. Except the chaos is watered down Up Front and the tactics are so absurd, it makes chess look like a realistic war game.
I get other games I don't like, but I will never ever understand the allure of this one.

RATING: 10
First game I bought after being away from wargaming for ~20 years. CC blew my mind as to what a wargame could be: fast, interactive, intuitive, and challenging. Time is elastic. Play the same scenario five times in a row and it’ll turn out different every time due to the cards, events, and variable objectives. Near infinite replayability with the Random Scenario Generator, not to mention the 100+ scenarios available between the base games and expansions. The rules and mechanics are ridiculously tight. And best of all, it generates sticky narratives unlike any game I’ve ever played. A masterpiece.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I keep trying to find something I don't like about this game and I can't. My first 10. 

This game isn't as random as many people make it out to be. With proper hand management and a little bit of card counting you'll find that you'll be able to do much of what you want to do. Personally I find the mechanics for hand management (playing actions, skipping turns to discard) very stimulating. That's not to say it's not random at all, just that the randomness is very well implemented and you can mitigate it with smart play. Most games where both sides make good decisions seem to come down to the wire which often won't happen in a truly random game.

I really like the fluid, sometimes non-linear way that the game models time and to some extent location. I also love how the card play creates a sense of uncertainty for both players. Nice fog of war.

Between fire/maneuver, hand management, bluffing, multiple victory conditions, random game length, and scenario special rules there are a ton of things to consider every turn which makes this a very engaging, satisfying system. You can keep playing and learn something every time you play. With all that plus a ton of scenarios in the expansions and the random scenario generator it also has lots of breadth.

The Initiative Card is amazing and I wish more games were aware of how swingy their dice results can be and included something similar to mitigate extremes.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I'm not a great fan of tactical level wargames, though Combat Commander nearly made a convert of me.  Despite the 24-page rules booklet, programmed learning makes it a very accessible game. There is also a strong narrative feel, mostly generated by the fate cards. 

That said, the level of randomness in Combat Commander bothers me.  I understand the point of it being there; to snap us out of the perfect knowledge and planning of earlier generations of tactical games.  Yet random events, secret objectives, uncertain game length and the luck of the card draw take the game too far towards the opposite extreme for my taste.  Added to that, those unpredictable events are sometimes simply odd. 

Production quality and artwork are pretty much standard GMT.  However, the counters are rather thin. On one game's evidence the rules seem uncommonly complete.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Game literally driven by cards. Making a strategy or tactical plans are useless.

I experienced this game as extremely annoying. Almost driven by luck. I have seen too often situations where I or my opponent don't get the cards I or he desperately needed. You need to move and no move cards show up, but have a hand full of fire cards but no targets to fire upon. This kind of situations happen too often to my taste.

Or what about this situation. Your best leader - the one you can't afford to lose - get suddenly stuck in a wire - an event played by the opponent. The only way out is to play an advance card, which you don't have and don't get. Then suddenly out of nowhere a paratrooper is in your back. Again an event played by the opponent. You're stuck while the enemy is firing and there is nothing you can do about it. This is clearly no strategy game, where the cleverest will win!

The designer gives credits to many games, except the game from which it is clearly derived ... (Advanced) Squad Leader.

RATING: 10
The best squad-level wargame. And all expansions are good.
This is actually the best game in my collection.

I've been searching for the perfect game of tactical combat and Combat Commander is the closest I got from finding perfection.

Things I like about CC:

1. Easy rules. It takes 4 or 5 plays for you to internalize them all. But after that, you don't forget them anymore;
2. You can't do everything anytime you want. The card driven order and action mechanisms allow for some command confusion in the heat of battle, and that is a big plus in my book;
3. The random effects and events. I love them, for they will assure that no game will be equal to the last;
4. Immense variety of (beautiful) maps and (well designed) scenarios;
5. It really tells a story.

Better than... Tide of Iron, Lock'n'Load, ASLSK, Conflict of Heroes, Sergeants, Heroes of Normandie.

A freaking Masterpiece.

I think I've experienced enough to know that it's probably not for me.  I actually enjoyed the game deck, compared to other card-activated games like M44 and the like (which I tend to dislike).  Still strongly prefer area attacks/movement rather than LOS, and still feel - perhaps amusingly - that Axis & Allies miniatures is easily my favorite of the LOS lot that I've experienced.  I'd read up and give it another go sometime, though, just in case. Update after a second play: still like, but don't love. Impressive ability to jump right in and "layer" complexity. Love the deck-does-it-all mechanics. Units are just flat for me, though, especially the lack of differentiation with things like armor. Leader activation also a bit of a drag. Still happy enough to revisit.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Absolutely fantastic. Plays like a movie. Perfect interaction of strategic card play, board play tactics, luck and theme.

RATING: 4
+ Nice production. Big maps, big chits, nice cards. I don't like the result, but I can't deny this is a creative design.
- It's much more of a cardgame than a wargame. Yes, I understand it's supposed to be representing "the chaos" in battle, but there are much better mechanics available in other games. It's a perfect example of the worse card-driven wargames can be, with all the randomness and lack of control. 
When I found myself using cardgame tricks such as exhausting decks while paying close to no attention to what was happening on the board, I realized I have no interest in what this game offers.

RATING: 8.25
Bit of a tricky sucker to get into....almost as confusing as the original Up Front rulebook. But if you've played ASL and UF you'll get this pretty quickly, and have a wild time playing.

I'm bemused by some of the detractors comments on this one...I've been playing ASL and Up Front for over 20 yrs, so can see the pedigree in this one...sure, there are at times a series of absolutely crazy events and situations that occur and as a player it may seem that you have limited control over what is going on...but read some small unit action reports and see just how much control a platoon commander had on what was going on around him. It's not better or worse than ASL, it's simply a different game and I enjoy playing it on a regular basis, but for me ASL is the ultimate WW2 small unit game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Tentative rating after 2 plays. What I like about the game is that is that it is light enought that you can learn the game and become good at it without having to own the game and spend hours learning it.  This is a game that a non wargamer could learn and become proficient at it in an afternoon.  The game flows smoothly and rapidly, you do not spend a lot of time looking combat results up on tables.  Much meatier than Memoir, but lighter that most wargames.  A wargamer may decry the game, and no, it isn't ASL, but then again it wasn't meant to be.  

It doesn't and shouldn't have tanks, despite the repeated requests.  That would horribly break a fine playing system.  If you'd like you can mimic the effect by saying the enemy assault gun is just sitting off the edge of the board out of range, and it slowly picks your side to pieces, just like round after round of artillery fire will do.

I think that a lot of people are going to be burning the the scenarios pretty quickly.  I can see small expansions or third party maps and/or scenario kits, the same as ASL.  I'm not sure what the replayability will be after you've played each scenario a half dozen times.

Like most wargames only really playable with 2.  Solitaire suitability medium.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The first time I played this game I didn't enjoy it, because some situations can develop where you can literaly do nothing, and it can be very frustrating (both my opponent and I spent some minutes just looking at each other and discarding, looking for cards that we could use); further plays have shown more variability and I've come to enjoy the game much more. The huge amount of scenarios available is a big plus.

The cards are a very interesting and innovative mechanic, contributing to fog of war and adding cool events and battlefield chaos. But they also mean that sometimes luck can play a big role. The game can be very fun and engaging when things are flowing well. Sometimes, however, the cards simply refuse to come out and then the best that can be said about it is that it's very frustrating. Fortunately the scenarios are short enough that you can simply give up and start over.

Strangely enough, I discovered that it actually plays well solo. Against an opponent, having him waltzing back and forth across open ground right in front of my heavy machine gun position, while there's nothing I can do about it because in 25 cards I didn't get a single fire card, is deeply infuriating; playing solo it's all happily part of the narrative, which is by far the strongest point about this game.

RATING: 6

If I want to play a WW2 tactical game which takes 1.5-3 hours I know ASL Starter Kit will better reward the time it takes to learn the system and study the tactics.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Mivel a Dominant Species számomra az egyik legjobb eurogém, sokat vártam a szerző leghíresebb wargame-jétől. Éveken át várta a szekrényben, hogy kipróbáljuk, de csak most szántam rá magam. Pedig így utólag annyira nem vészes a szabálya, és elolvasva még inkább pozitív várakozással néztem a játék elé.

A legegyszerűbb küldetést próbáltuk, és 2 óra alatt ment le, a szabálymagyarázat szerintem nem volt több fél óránál, mivel ebben a küldetésben még a tüzérség nem jut szerephez (a tüzérségi akciók kicsit komplexebbek, de azok se vészesek).

Pozitívumok: nagyon sok olyan ötlet van benne, ami tetszett. A legjobb a nyilvános és titkos célok megoldása, Eurokból jól ismert játékvégi célkártyák mintájára, de valahogy itt letisztultabban jelenik meg. Konkrétan arról van szó, hogy 5 objektum van a pályán, amiknek a birtoklásáért pont járhat, de a legtöbb pályán nincs teljes információnk arról, melyikért mennyi jár. Néhány egyéb szabálymódosítás is lehet a célok közt (pl. több pont jár a megölt egységekért). A másik ami tetszett, az az egyik játékvégi feltétel egyedi megoldása a time jelzővel. A kártyapaklik pedig itt végre nem 55 kártyából állnak:) És a többfunkciósság ellenére sincsenek túlbonyolítva a lapok. A kockadobás is a kártyákkal van szimulálva (ez is jó) és a 4 féle speciális dolog (pl. mesterlövész, time jelző léptetés...) bekövetkezése is  működik. Sok játéknál látom azt, hogy a nagy újrajátszhatóság érdekében csomó kieget kiadnak és olyan modulokkal pakolják tele, amik biztosítják a sokszínűséget. Nos itt szinte végtelen parti lehet, mert a 100+ féle térképhez nem csak a gyári kiegekben kiadott küldetések állnak rendelkezésre, hanem generálni is lehet külöböző kezdéseket. (Tudom, hogy ez wargame-eknél nem egyedi, de ez azok közé a wargame-ek közé tartozik, amikhez irtó sok pályát és kieget kiadtak. Ennek oka gondolom az, hogy nem 100 km-es léptékű és éveken át tartó háború zajlik egy partiban, hanem párszáz méteres terepen küzdünk végig és az időtáv is rövid, így a világháború akár minden napjából lehet keresni egy csatát, és így lehet újabb és újabb pályákat kiadni. Ez a játékcsalád ezt a lehetőséget maradéktalanul ki is aknázza. Egy kicsit a Memoirhoz hasonlít ebből a szempontból.)

És ezek fényében tudom is mondani az egyetlen nagy negatívumot, ami végül is nem is a játék hibája: nem lesz kivel játszanom. Most felkészültem belőle, játszottam vele egyet, de az ellenfelem nem nagyon esett hanyatt tőle, és 1-1 alkalmat leszámítva mást se fogok tudni leültetni hozzá... Állandó partner híján meg annyira nem könnyű azért, és a 600+ jelölőjével, 20+ oldalas szabályával nem az a játék, amit bármikor előveszek, még ha néha kapható is lenne rá valaki. Így annak ellenére, hogy a játékot szinte egyáltalán nem ismertük ki, a többszáz közül a legkönnyebb pályát próbltuk, pusztán néhány fajta egységet megismerve, szégyenszemre el fogom adni. Azért a legtöbb Euronál néhány játék elég arra, hogy kipróbálja az ember a teljes játékot, lássa az összes kártyát, minden szabállyal találkozzon. Itt tucatnyi játék kellett volna csak az alapdoboz küldetéseihez.

Ráadásul, ha már wargame-ezni szeretnék, van pár olyan, aminek sokkal előbb nekiülök(C&C, War of the ring).

Összefoglalva: szerintem jó játék, de sajnos ez ráérős nyugdíjasaknak való szöszmötöléssel jár, csak annak ajánlom, akinek kevés játéka van, és rá tudja szánni az időt hogy rácuppanjon, van partnere, akivel akár heti/havi rendszerességgel játszhatják. Azért pl. egy Paths of gloryhoz képest mindenképp könnyednek számít, tehát egy nap alatt simán le lehet nyomni belőle 2-3 partit is. Hajrá!

Majd egyszer valamikor talán újra próbálkozom ha több időm lesz:) Tuti hogy ez még 20 év múlva is kapható lesz, elég meghatározó a maga műfajában.

RATING: 7
I love Jensen's Dominant Species, and I enjoy the concept behind Urban Sprawl. Of course Combat Commander is quite a different game...

CC:E is a squad-level tactical wargame, with a simple card-driven mechanic, that wonderfully simulates the randomness and craziness of the battlefield.

Chad Jensen packed everything needed in each deck of cards: orders, events, dice rolls, machine guns' jams... The design is so neat and clever that even non-wargamers can pick it up quite fast!

CC:E is not a game for control freaks! Sometimes a random sniper shot or event can swing a scenario, and other times a lucky shot can make the difference between victory or defeat. But the game is tense and fun, in a "you can feel the battle in your guts way"!

Can't wait to play it again!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6.5
Bought at Tin Soldier closing down sale.

After 1 play:
Coming from ASL it's hard to like this game. The cards produce some really wacky behaviours - walking wounded in particular, Smoke doesn't dissipate for turn after turn and then goes right when you need it. Snipers are either irrelevant or devastating. The out and out randomness is a real turn off, not because it's "unrealistic" (an irrelevant consideration) but because it either feels like a distraction from the game or it impacts it oddly - this unit is a veteran, suddenly you have five more units when you just want the game to end etc.

For all the self-appointed pro players' assurances that hand management will make this next problem go away, I can't really get into the order system with such a small hand. You often need two move and two fire cards to do what you want to do, and you have between 4 and 6 cards. Not good odds, considering the other 7 types of card in the deck. It means long turns of nothing except drawing, which can be devastating if your opponent keeps drawing fire cards and you're in their range. A hand size of 7-10 seems more appropriate at first blush.

The end result was gameplay that felt clunky and unrefined. But I'll certainly give it another go to see if it gets better after a few plays.

The production values, on the other hand, are through the roof. I especially like the tracking sheet - open,e asy to read, easy to navigate and update. The maps are pretty good, too, and the counters are amazing.

You can see ASL in this game's lineage, from borowed terms to borrowed mechanics. There are parts of the game quite clearly put into it to avoid the rules overhead or complexity of ASL - the devastatingly deadly melee rules, for example, or the artillery or sniper rules. A big part of my problem is that I really like the way all of those rules play in ASL, so ultimately the rules overhead is worth it in my case. This game will never hit the table if I have the option of ASL, but givent he difficulty of getting others interested in ASL there is a place for this on my collection.

After 2 plays:

OK, I'm beginning to feel the hype. Once you get the rules down and start to understand how it all flows, it's really pretty good. I still really dislike anything with a random hex - it feels ridiculous. But the mroale and FP systems are good, Artillery is greatly streamlined over ASL (a good thing) and I'm even starting not to miss tanks. After a couple more games I'll rank this one, but I"m thinking around an 8.

After 5 plays: We were getting a LOT of rules wrong, and now I'm really beginning to get it - each unit only being allowed to activate once (the main rule we'd missed) really mixes things up and lessens the impact of not getting what you want in a given draw. Fixing that rule suddenly means it feels like each turn is only a few seconds, rather than along, drawn-out deckbuilding slugfest. Getting CC:M has also added a lot of replayability to the game. Looking forward to trying out some platoon-sized random scenarios.

I will say that I don't like the lack of memory that the game has - walking wounded and random-hex blazes are good examples - ASL is complex because it remembers EVERYTHING you do, which takes time. CC:E remembers nothing - not where units were when they died, not where your shells fall, not who moved last turn... It makes for a very odd game at times, and I'll never really get over that.

EDIT: ASL is climbing in my personal esteem again, and CC:E is being edged out - the main thing that has turned me off it is the realisation that leaders stack morale bonuses, so invincible kill stacks are too easy to construct. This sort of thing makes it all a bit boring. While I can see the point to keeping this in my collection to play when a non-ASL player wants a fast WW2 tactical game, I would happily trade it away to get some shelf space back - I really only need one WWII tactical and ASL is it.

RATING: 7
Solid, squad-level WWII wargame. Does an excellent job of creating a detailed, tactical combat narrative (story) without the necessity of learning and correctly implementing equally detailed rules found in nearly all other squad-level wargames. Play time will vary for players depending on how much of the rules set that they can grasp. That is, players familiar with the concepts found in Squad Leader, Up Front, and Ambush! should warm to this game and play should move quickly. Players not so familair (or rusty from long absence, like me) may take a bit more time to find their groove. I think, with practice, games should finish in about two hours. Still slow for players that enjoy most euros or battle games like Memoir '44, but a reasonably fast and action-packed two hours for genuine wargamers. 

The price for playing a game where a lot of the detail is handled by a (IMO, superlative) set of Fate Decks is "control": control over your units and the things that happen to them. And if you are a wargamer who thinks that the actions and events generated by the Fate Decks in this game are true to your understanding of WWII warfare, you will probably enjoy that a lot of fiddly detail is taken off your shoulders. But if you don't think that the Fate Decks do a good job of recreating the events you read about in after-action reports and the like, then the game may come across as a tedious card-pulling excercise. 

For all the detail in the Fate Decks, however, the battlefield narrative feels a bit lacking in some respects. For example: there are no vehicles--of any kind--in the game. Not even lowly horsecarts, trucks, or jeeps. Ironically, there are airplanes buzzing overhead that make fleeting and potentially painful appearances. And there is off-board artillery (which are probably being pulled by the missing horse-carts, jeeps, and trucks) that will thunder in when called. Such a lack is not necessarily "wrong" from a game standpoint, but it does feel a bit odd to move down roads that have no vehicles (not even as the detritus of war) and into and past houses that shelter no people. In this respect, it has some of the alien feel of a first-person shooter or other 3-D TacSim computer game. It's just you, the bad guys, and your weapons. 

Overall, my impression is favorable. It's nice to get a meaty Squad Leader fix without also learning a ream of rules and dedicating a long afternoon or evening to play.

RATING: 9
Even though I'm not a great fan of tactical games (prefer strategic level), this game is great. It feels like a mix of Memoir'44 and Squad Leader, you control squads but with a card-driven engine. There are 12 scenarios, but they are quite replayable because of all the random events that crop up no game seems to play the same.

I thought the lack of geomorphic maps would be a bit of a limitation - but having 12 specific maps is great, especially like that LOS is the actual line traced so you can fire between buildings etc

The game can be a bit of a culture shock for wargamers used to a more structured form - you can't always move all your units, you can't always fire. You have to hold the right cards, so part of the game (like Memoir'44) is managing your hand, getting the right cards to launch your assault. It's also possible to make a reckless move and pull it off, if your opponent is lacking in fire cards. Get your head around the system and it works great.

Only downside for me is it's currently limited to just German, US and Russian forces (need the expansion to add in the Axis minor allies, Italians, Brits)

RATING: 9
CC:E was the first real wargame I ever played.  For quite a while I'd sworn off wargames - too complex, too much die-rolling, and too much conflict.  Well, we finally got to a point where we needed more complexity in our games, and having exhausted most of the heavier Euros, we decided on this one after I'd been taught it elsewhere.  We wanted the experience of Memoir, with more grit and control, and CC delivers in spades.

What do I like best about CC?  The fact that you have to work to win.  It feels like war - you're angling for any advantage you can get.  I love the way the deck works, as both a timer, a random number generator, and as the main action mechanism.  The rules, while robust, are also fairly straightforward, easy to learn, and ensconced in a great, easy to reference rulebook.  The game has many scenarios, and I like that there are multiple paths to victory every time.  Plus, the random scenario generator is so much more than just random scenarios - it basically lets you craft a homebrew scenario for almost any theater and/or mode of combat.  

Having played and enjoyed both this and SoS, the edge goes to CC.  Why?  It just feels the most "real."  It simulates the chaos of combat better than any game I've played, and you feel connected to your men in a way you don't in more abstracted wargames.  If you have an interest in WWII and simulations based thereon, CC:E will certainly satisfy.

RATING: 3
Downgraded my numeric rating to a 3 to more accurately reflect how I feel about this game.
---
UPDATE: While I think the core ideas in Combat Commander are interesting, after playing it half-a-dozen times it just failed to inspire me at all. The not-quite-functional morale system, the ineffectiveness and awkwardness of firepower, the overwhelming chaos of the random events, but above all, the frequently tedious pace of the game combine to weigh Combat Commander down heavily. It's now on my "to liquidate" pile, which irritates me, because a) I bought it, and b) it's yet another expensive GMT game with huge promise that just can't deliver on its potential. GMT's lineup now seems to consist largely of this sort of game. 
---
So I've ended up playing CC:E half-a-dozen times now, and I'm still not quite sure what to make of it. It's a very odd design. For a simple game, the rules are baroque and bizzarely-presented, and there is still just too much junk in the system. Each deck has like 15 rout cards in it, but their application is so rare that it's kinda weird (in my games, they are usually played just to clear a card knowing nothing could possibly happen). Rally rolls are frequently automatic or nearly so. Even when you have lots of firepower you usually end up making many low-odds attacks due to the way the system heavily penalizes large firegroups. Doing anything requires far too many steps; the fire combat system - the core of the game - is tedious, especially in light of how infrequently it generates results (random events seem more frequent than effective fire). And the truly bizzare (and suspension-of-disbelief breaking) happens with too much regularity; CC:E makes Up Front seem like a hard simulation (in a recent 2.5 hour game, my German assault force suffered no losses at all to enemy fire, but had two squads eliminated by random event). And oh yeah, it seems like the scenarios might have some balance issues.

It's still kind of a fun game for what it is, though, although a bit too long for what you get out of it (not surprising in light of the above comments, probably). But the current high rating shows how much standards have fallen in wargames since the old AH quit the field and many wargamers defected to eurogames.

CC:E definitely feels, to me, like most recent GMT games. It's done by a first-time design team with apparently minimal development support. It feels like there are great ideas in there, but it also feels like it needed an experienced hand to guide it along, to file off a bunch of the rough edges, to get the pacing and play time of the game right, to manage the production elements to make sure the package wasn't too expensive, and to and to generally really turn the screws on the whole thing. CC:E is definitely OK and at least some fun, but it could possibly have actually been a *great* game with a strong and experienced developer; as it is, it's pretty far from there. I think the designer would have been better advised to try to aim for a smaller game and to get it right.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
There are few games that I've obsessed about as much as I have this one and, even though I've played it only a handful of times, I can't imagine anything better.  The whole package is one big pile of goodness, a rich and engaging narrative that unfolds through the play of your cards.  Few games will generate the kinds of memorable moments that this game will.

RATING: 3
I am an ASL player and initially thought CC:E would be a great game to introduce Eurogamers and wargame newbies to tactical WWII games. I couldn't be further wrong. 

CC:E is a very luck-dependent game. It is basically a card game with a board and cardboard counters to give it the grognard look. The card game mechanics basically involve playing one or two cards per turn and discarding cards in turns where your hand is crap and waiting for a good combos to use all in one turn. Getting the combos does not mean your intended actions will succeed, if your opoonent has the right card to counter your action. Imagine several turns of card discards and finally getting the combos required and doing some damage to the opponent's pieces, then the opponent shows a counter effect card which negates your actions.

The shooting mechanics, card response mechanics (to counter the other player's actions) and random event mechanics all contribute to what should be Up Front with a board, but, instead, resulting in a frustrating, chaotic, luck-dependent game enough to put newbies off wargames. You'd be better off playing M'44 with newbies. there is either something inherently wrong with CC:E's card mechanism, or there is something wrong with the mix of cards per say. The gameplay is terribly unexciting.

As for grognards, they will probably rather play ASL, or ATS, or anything less luck-dependent and chaotic. 

Update: Downgraded from 5 to 3.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I'm not normally into tactical games, despite my general interest in wargames. Computer tactical wargames, such as Combat Mission, simply do such a superior job at solving the problems that are inherent to the genre that tactical board games just don't interest me. 

Tactical board games always make the mistake of either avoiding any attempt to solve these problems, ending up being too "gamey" and lacking too much in simulation (e.g., Memoir '44) or they try too hard to solve these problems in a misguided attempt to match the level of simulation that only a computer can acheive (e.g., ASL).

But Combat Commander strikes me as a reasonable balance between "game" and "simulation" for a light wargame. True, you get a few anomolies, like sometimes being unable to fire at troops in the open for lack of any "Fire" command cards, but it may actually have fewer major anomolies than ASL and its ilk in some cases. ASL seem to me to focus too much on physics (which way is the wind blowing? How hard? Looks like your smoke is clearing) and not enough on fundamentals such as command and control, schwerpunkt, fog of war (missing from Combat Commander as well), simultaneous movement, etc. But Combat Commander at least tries to solve some of these as best a light war game probably can. Overall, its a decent beer & pretzels game that's about on par with Command & Color: Ancients.

UPDATE: Played Up Front and found it to be the superior game. You would think that adding a map would be an improvement but, surprisingly, it isn't. Adding the map takes what is otherwise a good card game and a reasonable simulation (given that so much is abstracted) and breaks both, turning it into a nonsensical luck fest in comparison.

UPDATE: I've decided that CC is not only too random  but that it is random in such a way that it hurts CC both in terms of compelling game play and in terms of depicting realistic results. Sold.

RATING: N/A
Acquired, opened, but not played.  Just didn't get around to it, and with the introduction of the Band of Brothers series, now I won't devote the time.  Based on my experience with other tactical-level, WWII infantry games:

For a game about command-and-control of an infantry company, play Fields of Fire.

For a card-driven game about fog-of-war, play Up Front.

For a streamlined, fast-playing game about fire-and-movement, play Band of Brothers (Screaming Eagles, Ghost Panzer, etc.).

Of course, for nth-degree detail, and the best simulation of a WWII movie, play Advance Squad Leader.

RATING: 2
three plays 2 player

Combines the best/worst of card driven - hand management games into the traditional WWII tactical combat space.  It's an interesting and innovative mix.  No more or less "realistic" than other games in the genre, but more dramatic than the SL/ASL take.

On the down side, there is a learning curve, and you can find yourself discarding again and again trying to get the cards you need.  It is not a quick game, in fact it could be described as overly long for the payoff.  The rule complexity remains considerable - it has simply been distributed between the rulebook, half a dozen player aides and the cards.

Contrary to what I've read, one can apply and must apply sound tactical principals and methods to do well ... but you do so within the confines of the card driven system.  So for example, if you're defending you want to be sure to keep "Fire" cards in your hand so you'll be able to ops fire.  At the same time, it is artificial that a unit needs such a card to Ops fire at all ... but every game has abstractions.

Update after two more plays.  CC:E seems to be forcing the card driven mechanic on us so that one never fully suspends their disbelief that it's all just a game.  I suspect this title appeals to fans of card games, but not those who are looking for reasonable historical accuracy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
:tobacco::(:DMy experience with this title consists of two games of Scenario #1, so I speak from the highest authority on Combat Commander, much the same way that someone who's rifled through the SparkNotes to Merchant of Venice is an authority on English lit. That said, reasoned judgement born of long experience is for wankers. So.

Much as I generally love wargames, wargames at the tactical level have always been in a dubious cool-in-theory/annoying-in-practice place in my mind, not helped by the fact that there seems to have historically been a "how much baroque complexity can we stuff into this package" contest amongst designers of tactical games on the one hand, and fun-enough-but-hardly-groundbreaking stuff like Memoir '44 on the opposite pole. The search for the happy medium brought me belatedly to Combat Commander.

That's 'bout right.

Start with the design -- what we have here is a rock-solid set of rules aided and abetted by a rock-solid manual. I'm still hazy on some rules but the ease with which I found answers in the book leaves me with more of a sense than usual that any rules mistakes I'm making are my damn fault. Let us now praise famously well-written manuals! And one suspects that it's no coincidence that another rules-heavy game by the same designer has a similarly airtight rulebook. But this one leaves even Dominant Species' book in the dust, in part because it's rising to an even greater challenge of complexity, and in part because it neatly balances disarming chattiness that reduces the angst of absorbing said complexity with actually having the information you need where you need it. In my last game, I had to look something up 6-7 times, and each time the question I was asking was unambiguously answered in the first place I looked.

To me, the card play (as nutso as it makes people who feel a not-unrealistic sense of entitlement to move all their dudes every time it's their turn) makes the game work very well as a game. It has an underappreciated simulation value as well -- on top of requiring players to manage events and strike a difficult balance between managing the risk of holding back and waiting for the perfect hand vs. the risk of pulling off an imperfect maneuver and possibly getting reduced to kibble by those entrenched, it also conveys the desired tone of battlefield chaos, and it also neatly addresses (albeit in an abstract way) a big problem with this sort of game, which is to say: fog of war. You can see where everyone is all the time -- in a 30,000 foot grand strategic game like Virgin Queen, the answer is, you know, who cares, of course you know where people are. In a game like this, where ambushes and uncertainties are the name of the game, the inherent limitations of a single central map and pieces that must be moved around means that the uncertainty has to come from somewhere. The cards make that work.

Another thing: this game is not (usually) a simple matter of marching guys up to shooting range and plugging away at each other. Killing enemy troops is [i]hard[/i], unless they're standing in the middle of a field picking their noses, and you're unlikely to get such a blessing unless your opponent has had a lousy run of cards. You've got to build a hand that can really make a difference. If you're simply playing an Order, or even two Orders in your turn, you're likely to be faffing around. What you want is SICK CARD COMBOS BRAH. Orders combined with Actions, that's where you start making a real difference. And that means building a hand, which means there's some turns where you're just discarding and hoping to get what you really want.

I am increasingly pleased with this as a game; there's real layers that I've only just started to pull back, and there's a ton to explore in this box.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
A very fun game.  While it has some unpredictable elements, I like the system immensely.  I question the "realism" of the game, but then again, in actions this small, anything really could happen.  It also gives one hope, even if you're losing badly.  This game is a keeper, and I plan on purchasing the expansions as well.

UPDATE 06/29/10: After over a year since my last comments above, I am falling for this game more and more.  The "realism" aspect has been defended by at least one military officer with small unit combat command experience.  I have been taking my time learning the game, playing through the base game scenarios first.  I am currently on Bonfire of the NKVD, having played the Russians.  I will play the German side in this scenario next.  It appears that the attacker has a longer learning curve in this game - both I and my opponents seem to have a tougher time winning scenarios as the attacker.

UPDATE: 11/29/10: I am upgrading my rating for this game.  It's definitely one of my favorite games to play.  Thanks, Chad and Kai!

RATING: 10
Combat Commander is a very fun system...and easy to get into. I haven't figured out the best way to store my components yet, for easiest set-up, so that part can feel a bit fiddly. However, I'm happy with the quality of those components.

I was surprised at how high the luck factor was in this game. I like that there are multiple endgame triggers, and multiple ways that time can advance (often unpredictably), which really ratchets up the tension in a close game. Also, I love the way events can change the complexion of a single game by introducing new objectives, bringing in reinforcements, etc.

This game creates a fabulous narrative. Its really fun to watch the way the game unwinds. I also enjoy the hand/deck management. It translates well to the CDG system. Again, I was a bit surprised to see how wide the swings of luck can be. Since playing a particular scenario can be fairly quick, this is tolerable. After just a few plays, I was convinced to order the expansion. I look forward to lots of plays here.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
COMBAT COMMANDER is an exceptionally well-done game that yes, does capture some of the cardplay elements of UP FRONT while adding a more specific sense of physical space, time, and scale.  The game uses cards for everything- orders, actions, events, and even die rolls- and I think that by and large the system works pretty well and allows for some interesting narrative and drama.  At first I thought some things were a little odd, like wire popping up out of nowhere but it makes sense when you think about the scale and how your men might just run into some wire they didn't expect before they advanced.  There is "chaos", whatever that means within a very strict system of rules, but it's very controlled and navigable when it occurs.  I think the game does run a little long per scenario but it could just be due to the "newbie" factor and ultimately I can't see it replacing UP FRONT as my game of choice for this type of game and I strongly suspect that TIDE OF IRON might steal a lot of its thunder in much the same way that BATTLELORE stole C&C:A's.  COMBAT COMMANDER is definitely close to greatness though- the production is really outstanding (although WAY overpriced at $80 retail)and it's an accessible and exciting game that I'd definitely like to see expanded upon and developed further- the game sorely needs armor!

Two players, playing 2 different powers, shouldn't spend the first half of any game, not able to move troop and funcion competently.  I can't imagine the furstration level that would come with playing France where I can only dump 1 card at time.

Although far more accesible and simpler than ASL, ASL its not.  this game was inspired by ASL, that I can tell, but give me ASL any day over this one.  I will continue to play this one, but I hope the cardplay stops being a hinderance to the game.  I don't mind the idea of confusion in the game, but it shouldn't last as long as it often does in CC.

(2 plays).  This is a good squad level game.  It mixed both wargame and a mix of "euro-ness" to it.  At times the cards become quite frustrating when you have cards but still cannot do a single thing.  The game length is a tad random, the events even moreso, but if you are looking for a bridge between Memoir '44 and ASL, this is the game for you.

RATING: 10
Combat Commander produces an excellent narrative, story-driven game every time I've played it. It is my favourite tactical WW2 wargame.

Why is it considered good? I think that Combat Commander is an example of a traditional tactical-level WW2 hex-and-counter wargame that manages to recreate fog of war through its innovative and perfectly balanced card-driven mechanic, reminiscent of Up Front. 

Many will complain the cards add too much randomness, but to my mind, this is one of the few ways that tactical WW2 wargames can most effectively implement fog-of-war and the chaos of a WW2 battlefield as well as can be expected in a hex-and-chit wargame. And far better a card-driven fog-of-war and friction than a 10,000 foot general who can make his cardboard chits do his bidding without question, or know precisely the odds of his forces being able to accomplish something like rallying when suppressed. 

Combat Commander forces you to adapt to the moment, to accept chaos and unpredictable events, and to simply seize and exploit unexpected opportunities on the battlefield better than your opponent.

The cards in Combat Commander aren't there to create a barrier to action, they simply introduce elements on a battlefield that are true to the era and genre. 

A squad that turns a corner and runs into barbed wire, a hidden minefield in front of the main defenders line, reinforcements appearing, stray rounds setting a building on fire, weapons breaking and getting repaired, the ammo runner who never shows up ... these are all things that are nothing unexpected in a game trying to recreate tactical combat, and always add a source of narrative, delight and fun, even when that wire or minefield is played on you. Mark Buetow says that this sort of thing "... strikes an ideal balance of playability, surprise, and visceral excitement." Yes, it does.

Events unfold on the battlefield as they should, and the gameplay will create a great narrative every time. Combat Commander effectively compresses platoon- and company-sized engagements, and the chaos, heroism and events that may have lasted half a day or more in 1944, into a two-hour session of elegant, fun gameplay. Impossible things happen like they did. Units can't fire effectively at a hero dashing across a field to take out a MG nest or two? Some complain that not having a fire card makes no sense, why can't my machine gun fire at that lone hero dashing across an open field ... and yet, that's how my father-in-law won his Cross of Valour. That it can occasionally happen in this game makes perfect sense because it did happen. The game produces realistic results through one of the best design for effect systems ever made for this scale of wargaming. Combat Commander provides "narrative realism" with ease, and offers players a simple but nicely implemented system to differentiate national doctrines and unit capabilities through the decks of cards that are built to be specific to different nations.

I also equate the lack of a fire card in your hand to the enemy successfully concealing himself in the terrain while moving.

The rules? GMT has produced what are, arguably, one of the genre's finest examples of clarity and simplicity. There is an ebb and flow to the game, and it plays so fast and smoothly it's a joy. Did I mention that Chad Jensen's rules are one of the best examples of wargaming rules ever written? The Random Scenario Generator is fantastic and offers Combat Commander fans infinite replayability.

Obviously, the game borrows from, and is inspired by, some of Up Front's elements which is both a good and bad thing for some war gamers (though some of what UF does simply can not get translated well to any game that uses a fixed map), but the spirit of amazing fun remains. Combat Commander creates a fantastic narrative and the system provides for a wide range of theatres and forces. Combat Commander has become, without question, the singular tactical level WW2 wargame I deeply enjoy playing.

Oh, yes ... no tanks? At the map scale that Combat Commander reproduces, and in these scenarios, I love that this game is bold enough to be just infantry. I prefer it, in fact. There are other good games that model the map distances needed for pure tank tactics if you're after armoured action at a tactical scale.

Any aspects of Combat Commander that I forgive because the game is otherwise brilliant? Four things:

1) There isn't any real focus on realistic command and control (outside of what the nation-specific card decks represent). But the only game at this scale that does was designed to be that: Fields of Fire. 
2) Morale and suppression are still the two-state model of broken or "eliminated" we've been using since the 1970s. 
3) Players do not need to use proper WW2 squad-level tactics to play well. 
4) You can play movement cards in excess of your nation's legal card discard limits by using these to move individual units out of, then right back into, cover. Like ASL skulking, this is perfectly legal but intentionally distorts the intent of the discard rules, and is patently gamey and silly to my mind.

Those minor points aside I still feel that Combat Commander is a brilliantly designed, simple-to-teach, fun-to-play, WW2 tactical wargame that offers an excellent narrative representation of WWII tactical combat.  

Despite being a long-time ASL player "back in the day", I personally find the "lack of the card I need" in Combat Commander versus the gamey loopholes and odd mechanics any good ASL player can exploit to be an entirely acceptable compromise. Tactical WWII wargamers should read Mark Herman's brilliant essay on why CDGs offer, in many cases, the best solution to countering perfect battlefield knowledge:

"Having the ability to survive and even prosper with a bad hand requires new skills beyond the ability to count hexes and calculate combat ratios. The ability to deceive your opponent by making an unusual play that brings fear and consterna- tion to your opponent brings the com- manders skills of bluff and deception to the forefront. CDGs bring uncertainty to the equation in such a manner that the ability to gain advantage by confusing your opponent becomes the game, just as it is in real warfare.

Where the CDG takes a new path is the player does not know what his opponent can do with those forces and where he intends to act." 

This I like. A lot. It represents exactly what real-world commanders have had to face in combat.

[b]I own and preorder ALL of the expansion(s) for this game:[/b]

[b]Combat Commander: Mediterranean[/b]
Combat Commander: Resistance
Battle Pack #1: Paratroopers
Battle Pack #2: Stalingrad
Battle Pack #3: Normandy
Battle Pack #5: The Fall of the West
Battle Pack #6: Sea Lion
Battle Pack #7: Leader of Men Tournament Battle Pack

RATING: 1
As a wargame, this title falls flat on it's face with absurd events and erratic and random gameplay. However, it does do something interesting: it makes euro-gamers 'think' they're playing a real wargame. It has the look of a wargame (counters and maps), but all the detail and tough decision-making have been rolled up into a deck of cards. For those who are cowed by making tough decisons - draw a card instead. For those willing to spend time after the game trying to rationalize it's events - sit right down and play. For those who feel that this game accurately represents the chaos of the WWII battlefield, I suggest you give electric football a try (http://www.boardgamegeek.com/game/7584) - a similar title boasting an equally accurate depiction of the chaos that occurs on a professional football field.

RATING: 7
Definitely a step up from Memoir '44, without devolving into the complexity of ASL. It's entirely card driven, though with plenty of chaos, too. You either embrace it or you don't. Fortunately for me, I was able to embrace it. The local squad level, card driven movement and attack mechanics make much more sense for this game than they ever did for C&C: Ancients.

The playing time is relatively short, there are lots of scenarios, you can learn the rulebook in stages, and there are lots of expansions to suit your tastes.

Update: after a few more plays, slight drop in rating. Partly this is because I've played more tactical WW2 squad level games since my original rating, and I've decided that I prefer stuff like [i]Conflict of Heroes[/i] or [i]Band of Brothers[/i] over this, as too much can hinge on the cards. Still, it's a good game and I'm happy to play someone else's copy from time to time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.75
Does a good job of representing the 'fog-of-war' and the uncertainties of command of the battlefield. It plays like a cinematic fire-fight and is more accessible than full ASL although it does have a couple of issues and I would still play ASL in preference for a number of reasons:-

- Luck and random factors play too much of a part in deciding the outcome. There are multiple layers of luck: do you have the right cards to take an action you want? does your opponent?; if you fire there is still the luck of a pulled dice roll; plus the layer of random events that can descend.....the latter are a big deal....
- .....they can be fun, and add to the chaos of war feel, but can be very random and game winners or losers, over which the players have no control.
- The system is just not as flexible as ASL, or capable of scaling up or down as well.
- Not as epic in the tales it tells as ASL
- No vehicles
- It's even more gamey and although it's gamey elements all fit together well and some may regard them as plusses, if you are hand managing and cycling a deck to end the game, the illusion of tactical combat is inevitably somewhat dispelled.


It's still a lot of fun and a terrific bit of game design - particular mention should go to  the VP objective mechanism which is very good, as is the attempt to reflect national command and control capabilities by the variable nationality fate decks .

RATING: 10
Wow!

I expected to be disappointed considering all the hype, but finally got around to playing this 10 times over a gaming retreat weekend (Jan 2008)and it's great!

As an early participant in Bruce Wigdor's Internet Up Front League. I'm a big fan of Up Front, but I must say that, as a tabletop game, CC:E is a much slicker product.

This game appears to have been an excellent development effort on top of a great design. Well done everyone involved!

It's a bit too "wargame-ish" (detailed rules, line of sight etc...) for your casual Eurogamer, but for the large number of people who like both wargames and "heavy" Euros it's a "must have" game.

Highly recommended.

Update: October 2008 - moved rating up from 9.5 to 10. Perhaps my favourite wargame of all time.

RATING: 6
Squad level World War 2 wargame.  Players are ... I guess platoon or battalion leaders sending their squads into action.  Players use cards to issue orders to units, which are carried out.  Orders may allow more cards to be played as actions, which enhance or respond to orders.  Units are leaders, squads and teams that are moved across the map.  As well as actions and orders, the cards are used to roll dice which can trigger various game functions (time advancement, events, etc).  Victory is points driven, and granted by holding objectives (which can be worth variable amounts of points), exiting your opponents map edge, and eliminating enemy units.  Nice game, lavish production from GMT, with the ability to play the US, German and Soviets.  The first scenario is a bit vanilla, but an excellent introduction.  The next couple offer tense and well balanced games.  A very nicely put together tactical system, with a strong emphasis on fun and narrative over realism.  Simple to pick up and play, offering some really touch choices via the card management issues, with clear and very well developed rules.  Excellent game (yes, "game"), but grognards stuck in the 80's will have a conniption.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I was very excited about the release of a new card-driven tactical game. But several major faults prevent me from liking this game. The game tries to use cards and leaders to simulate command and control limitations, yet even the most unwieldy of Russian recruits are very responsive. Having veterans just means fewer squads, not better command. 

The second major issue is that it fails use hand management effectively. Small hand sizes mean that dumb luck is more important than smart play. Up Front also used small hand size but often both players spent turns cycling cards. In Up Front both players are struggling at all times! 
 
However this game is a huge step forward in reducing the impenetrable wargame rulebooks. The designer took a page from CCG's and put the details on the cards! All of these chaotic details are elegantly handled via the event system and these succeed at adding a lot of flavor. The fun and elegance of this make this attractive to many new players, and I expect for most it will replace Squad Leader, ASL, Lock & Load, and similar games. 
 
Also, the designer did an amazing job with maps, play aids, and rules. So despite the fact it is a GMT game we found none of the glaring errors that have long plagued most of GMT's shoddy productions.

RATING: 8
2017-08-28 : First play. Sometimes you come across a game-system where all you really want to say is that it's a bloody neat game. I had my doubts before playing it because I love to chuck dice but the card system here simply works. I love how it allows events to happen at the best and worst of times, how it forces players to think on their feet and take a chance .. or not. The litmus test of every tactical wargame is always : does what happens in front of my eyes look and feels realistic - and the answer in an emphatic yes. Some people complain about not being able to opportunity fire at everything that pokes its nose out from behind a tree but that's just how it was : an enemy is not spotted in time, or just for 1 second while crossing a street and you're too late to react, or you're low on ammo or distracted by the firefight to your left or a hundred other things. War is managed chaos - and those that can manage it best will do well at this game.

RATING: 4
Chad Jensen has reinvented Avalon Hill's "Squad Leader" game, considered by many the gem of the tactical infantry combat genre, with an elegant simplicity.  This game is faster, and easier to learn and than Squad Leader, but, IMHO, sacrifices substantial realism and historical accuracy.  Card-driven mechanic seems to generate irrational results, like failure to fire on nearby enemy troops moving in the open.  Further, the game does not treat vehicles, which is a serious drawback in the opinion of this writer.  IMHO, the game's somewhat abstact scenarios detract from historical flavor. Recommended for players looking for a somewhat abstracted game of WWII infantry combat that can be learned, setup, played and put away in three hours.

RATING: 4
CC:E is a valiant attempt at creating a simple squad-level wargame but though it may look like a wargame but IMO, it's not. Or at least, this is about as a wargame as Memoir '44 - whatever CC:E may be simulating, it's not WWII squad-level combat. The game revolves around decision-making that doesn't put the player into a commander's shoes at all. It comes across mostly as a hand-management game than a squad-level tactics game. 

Uneven card mix is a serious issue. Some cards are just completely useless in some scenarios, which leads me to question the rigor of scenario design as well. And the right card at the right time can be seriously advantageous for a player who had up till then been outplayed by his opponent.

Overall, it feels at times like a badly-designed Euro game with a pasted-on theme - so incongruous is the game to what it is purportedly simulating.

RATING: 6
The core mechanism is order cards that one gets randomly from their own deck. You don't know what orders your opponent has or which ones you'll get next turn, so you're always feeling vulnerable. You might not be able to do something effectively or even at all if you don’t have the right cards. The result is both good (the tension of the uncertainty) and bad (a lot of hand cycling where you’re both fishing your respective decks for cards to move or fire). 

The game is supposedly inspired by Up Front which is the game I’d prefer to play before this one. Perhaps after I explore Up Front I’ll revisit this one and appreciate what the design is going for.

RATING: 10
Well, MultiMan Publishing, eat your heart out.  You sat all these years on the Up Front game reprint and now we have an alternative.  A fast, easy to learn and highly replayable alternative.  While the game is not perfect, it is a whole lot of fun.  And people: we have a rules index!!!  This is one learning curve I will gladly hike.

Note to GMT: in this day and age of digital printing presses and high quality specialized papers and cardboards, it is completely unacceptable to deliver a subpar product.  The die cutting seems to be the exact same one used by SPI and Avalon Hill way back when.  Half my counters were off-centered and I had to use scissors again to clip the annoying little bits at the corners, all the while wondering how come I had to pay more for this game than the top notch Phalanx products.  Plus, I know I have more expense coming my way as the cards definitely have to sleeved if they are to suffer the punishment that's coming to them.  I will play this game a heck of lot, but I'm not a fully satisfied customer.  There is no reason that the components can't be better than this.

Update: 25 games thus far and I've yet to be bored.  Outstanding and now a staple of my gaming time.

RATING: 6
Latest update:  I like it fine, but Up Front has more tension in the hand management because fire attacks are more lethal, especially at close range.  CCE makes you use Advance cards and melee combat to get anything done.  Bumping firepower stats would improve the tension in CCE, as it is it's not too frightening to venture out into the open with your squads...  This game borrows so much of the card play from Up Front that it's hard not to compare the two.

RATING: 6
[1 play] Like every tactical WWII game I've ever played, it suffers from the all to frequent scenario of running to the best position, sticking your thumb up your ass, and hoping for good rolls/card draws before you have all that much to think about.  In the end, it's this time "hoping" that doesn't do it for me in these games.  I want more time spent making things happen and less time waiting for them to happen.  Still, the cards can tell a pretty decent story, and from that perspective the game is great, though overshadowed by Up Front in my mind.

RATING: 4
This is a CDG version of Squad Leader, with touches of Hannibal. It does have its moments occasionally: it's simple and can be fast playing, with plenty of scenarios and top flight components, but its cards are much, much too restrictive, which can result in frustrating stoppages. Now, this is completely understandable in many situations, such as when organizing a charge over open ground, but not at all in others. For example, in a typical game you might find elite German paratroopers defending a stone house overlooking a road. Now, I understand that getting green Russian troops to charge down the road might take some doing, but should it also take the same effort to get the Germans to conduct opportunity fire, or the Russians to find cover? In this game the answer is yes. Without further orders the conscripts simply stop walking and just stand around in the open taking fire until ordered to hide. But, on the other hand, without orders (that you probably don't have and therefore can't give) the Germans might only look out the window and not do a thing as the Soviets saunter by.  

  In other words, it's not unusual for nothing to happen in the most critical situations for the rest of the game or - if you are really lucky - until the right card shows up. This could have been avoided by having each card be a default "dig-in" or "op fire" card (after all, how hard can it be to get elite troops to return fire when attacked), but instead we've been given a somewhat disjointed and occasionally aggravating experience. So, if you can overlook these flaws in the name of "realism" so-called then this game might work for you. Although, why people insist on thinking this game's strength is its "realism" when it does not take into account the effect of distance on accuracy, or the effect of self-preservation on decision making or the effect of troop quality on melee is beyond me. 

Bottom Line: If you like Card Driven Games, if you like tactical World War II games, if you like games with much more than their fair share of randomness and if you are not high strung then give this game a play. However, I’d strongly suggest that you stay with only those scenarios that allow a large number of cards per hand and a large number of discards because if you ever have to play a 4 or 5 card hand with a 1 card order or discard limit there is a better than even chance your head will explode from frustration.

Downgraded from 4.5 to 4.0. I really would rather not play this again, although I suppose it is possible that I could be talked into it (but only if the talker is armed).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
CC:E clearly does what it sets out to do: create a sort of unpredictable war situation where players establish a plan and hope they can manipulate orders and on-the-fly actions to turn the tide in their favour. Random events also occur, which introduces something missing from many wargames: fate. In that, Jensen has designed a very interesting wargame that is accessible for all types of heavy gamers.  Both games that I played of this were a bit of a let-down. I think we were expecting the card-play element to be much more exciting. Instead, I find the lack of options at times more frustrating than tension-building and the order mechanics not giving anything unique enough to make CC:E stand out for me. I think it's a great game for those who like it, and I don't doubt at all their enjoyment of it. But for my personal tastes, CC:E doesn't create nearly as much tension as the C&C or COH systems, which are medium wargames of the same ilk and two systems I very much enjoy. I'm certain that if we were to play this more we would find the necessary subtleties to bring us through, but I don't feel particularly motivated to explore CC:E when there are a few other games I love much more.

RATING: 7
This is pretty quick for a war game, and it is easier to teach than I would have expected. The swings of fortune can be extreme, but this adds a lot to the excitement and the replay value of the game.
The card-based actions work much better than in Command and Colors games. At the same time, they are simpler and more streamlined than something like Twilight Struggle or Paths of Glory.
I am loving this game more and more as I play. The crazy risks and gambles you can take, only to be butchered by heavy machine gun fire, really lends the game a storytelling aspect that is tons of fun.
Do yourself a favor, even if you aren't a wargamer, and get this game.

Update - Traded. It's still a good game, but hard to get to the table and hard to solo.

-----

I recently updated my rating from a 5, which was too harsh.  This is a fun game that is also fairly easy to get in to.  Text below was my initial assessment:

Gamey.  I found myself wishing there were some sort of protective fire provision that didn't require a fire card.  It just doesn't make sense to me that a squad would let the enemy move adjacent without putting up a fight.  I wonder if an optional rule could be implemented to account for this.  Something like final protective fire in ASL, which comes at a price: the firer must pass a moral check or break.  Yes, this very well could be a "hand management" issue on my part, but is this a game of battlefield tactics or card cycling?  In the end it seems that the game was designed around the card mechanic, with the tactics "shimmed" to fit.

I got all the expansions for the european line so I wont get Pacific (with all the stuff I have right now I can play it for years without getting tired of it). Components are just ok for the eurogamer but good compared to the standard wargame.

RATING: 10
First play = instant classic.  Back and forth play with fast moving mechanisms.  We did a number of things wrong on our first run through, but we had a blast.  Fun factor makes up for tanks being MIA.

Second play without errors...even better. Command and control (or lack thereof) handled nicely with "Fate" cards.  Speaking of the cards, these cleverly make it easier to play the game because they guide you through the various orders, actions and events.  Players are not constantly looking at charts or having to remember arcane rule exceptions. you can focus on the situation at hand with the added tension of not knowing your opponent's full capability.

Third play...wow.  Great decision points and TENSION before, during and after those decisions!  Still trying to apply other tactical game rules (not an issue with CC, just my brain confusing CC play with ASL doctrine), but I think CC's approach/rules finally registered with me.  10...love to play anytime...win or lose.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
WWII tactical gaming is not an area of special interest for me but I wanted to try something in the genre and CC: Europe was the one I opted for. I really enjoy hand management, narrative and smooth, quick play and this one fit the bill. Played only once so far but I can see already that it delivers on all these fronts and will be a winner with me, particularly as I do a lot of my gaming on Vassal and it has the most fully developed and featured module I've seen for any game.
Edit 11/6/17: I've played all published scenarios solo and really enjoy the game and the narrative it generates. I have no skin in the historical debate that rages around its implementation of command and battlefield chaos. I just enjoy it. I'd like to be able to play it face to face more often, and a lack of local opponent lowers its rating for me, but it works fine as one of my solo indulgences.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
CC:E is a very well designed game.  The designer has made intelligent choices in sacrificing the right amount of "simulation/realism" in order to achieve a higher degree of "playability".  Although different folks will have differing opinions as to the best levels of realism vs playability, I think that Mr. Jensen has hit the sweet spot.

RATING: 8.5
This is a very good CDG of tactical infantry combat.  As in all CDGs the luck element is in the card draw.  The skill comes from maximising the benefits of a good hand and minimising the adverse effects of a bad hand.  It is imperative to know when to discard cards in the game and avoid the lure of holding on to a card for possible future use instead of ditching it to get the chance of pulling a card that will be useful in the present.  My only complaints are the rulebook layout which I found counter-intuitive for learning the game and the maps which seem to be of indifferent quality.  However, the other components, in particular the cards and counters, are excellent.

RATING: 9
Very original. At first glance seems like an SL-esque game (in part because of the components), only that has a card-driven mechanics.

But it's really another sort of creature. It's wargame without a sequential time frame, where "turns" doesn't happen in fixed chunks of time, and where the actions that have place in different segments of the board are not necessarily sequential or parallel between them.

Many times this game is misunderstood. A sequence of "turns" that could exhaust half of your deck, could really mean only few seconds of real action in a specific zone of the map (while you build your appropriate hand for dealing with a situation).

This misunderstanding leads frequently to criticisms like "Why do I need a fire card to fire?", "Suddenly I 'remembered' that I can do spray fire!", "I'm parked in the middle of the road with an enemy machine gun nest in front of me, and nothing happens!", and so on.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
A card-driven tactical wargame with a huge random factor. For starters it's infantry only, there are no vehicles and that sharply limits the variety of the scenarios. My most serious complaint, though is that the balance of the card decks feels quite wrong. There aren't nearly enough 'move' and 'rally' cards and your hand quickly fills with useless junk like 'rout' and 'commander confusion' (Just what was the point of including a card like that?). A simple improvement would be to halve the size of each draw deck, then you'd get the cards you want quicker and the game would be shorter, and don't even get me started on concepts like wire appearing by magic at some random point on the map.

Finally--- It is my favorite game of all time, so it has to be a 10.....

RATING: 8
Comparisons with ASL and Up Front (UF) are inevitable, as it is a bit like a combination of the two.   If you have played either ASL or UF, the game is easy to learn.  For me, CC does not replace UF or ASL, although I think that UF will get somewhat less play in favor of CC.   UF is more portable, requires a smaller playing area, plays in a shorter time than CC, and hand management is a more critical part of UF.  ASL is infinitely more comprehensive than CC.  While CC is limited to one map infantry scenarios, ASL can cover anything from small scenarios up to huge campaigns on large historical maps.  Also, every aspect of WWII land warfare is there.  For example, one glaring omission of CC is the lack of armored vehicles (Heck, even UF has armored vehicles).  On the other hand, CC purposefully focuses on WWII "infantry" combat and does an excellent job.  The game is fast, fun and wild, with frequent events mixing things up (CC plays more chaotically than either UF or ASL, but in an exciting and fun way).  CC is a big step up from Memoir 44, but a lot less complicated than ASL or UF, with many of the details of UF and ASL abstracted away, or made into triggered events.  (Based on 25+  games played so far).

RATING: 8.5
I normally frown heavily on randomness, high luck or especially chaos in most cases. I knew exactly what I was getting into with this and in a 2 player game not running over two hours I can deal with some of the above if the game makes up for it in FUN, well this game delivers. 

TONS of randomness and well most of it I'm just fine with, at least for now as honestly I think that is a big part of WHY the game was fun.  There seemed to be enough all over the place (random events, snipers & troops parachuting in at random locations, victory points, etc) that I think it evened out over the course of a game less maybe card draw. Also, most of it was themactic, added flavour and was good at simulating the feel of the true chaos of real war. 

Rolling dice by flipping cards was better than actually rolling dice I think and I also really like the initiative card to help mitigate a really bad roll at the wrong time, won’t always work but at least there is that chance, especially on a really bad roll.  So while I like the card flipping I DON'T like using your deck of much needed cards to do the dice rolling as you can burn though some of your much needed cards and this is really the randomness that kills you the most, not having the right cards at the right time which is something I didn’t like about Borg C&C systems. I would rather use a seperate dice deck for this but then that would kill the random event system and time etc, you really need two more duplicate decks to do this right, one for drawing and one for rolling, anyway it works fine and I've come up with an alternative fix. 

Overall a great little system and a fun game.  Lots of tough decisions to make and lots of strategizing in the game that I found subtle on first blush.  Tons of replay built into the system with 12 scenerios and each time you play a given scenrario it could play very differently depending on many factors.  Great semi-entry level (a little more than entry level) hex and counter which was my first ‘real’ war game played (although Duel of Ages was years ago and is more of a war game than most people think), after my first game which I thoroughly enjoyed I thought about it for a solid week after so that really says something as it rarely happens.  


Tidbit - Thinking about letting Attacker discard 4 cards to make any one and Defender discarding 2 or 3 for the same effect to help mitigate bad card draw instead of just possibly re-drawing the same crap and losing a turn (not tested).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Perhaps the most difficult factor to simulate in a board wargame is fog-of-war. It can be difficult to disguise the location, identity and condition of on-map units without onerous record-keeping. Block games do a good job of disguising unit identity and condition, except that the owner still knows exactly what to expect from his own people. Chit-based activation is another good mechanic for increasing the uncertainty inherent in the battlefield. Combat Commander approaches fog-of-war from a completely different angle: the cards. The cards in CC:E simulate fog-of-war because players can never be sure that "what you see" (units on the board) will translate into "what you get" (the ability of those units to receive or carry out your orders). One example: it might look like your rifle squad is 3 hexes from a key objective, but if you just can't draw a "move" card after repeated attempts, that squad might actually have started 10 or 15 hexes away from where you thought it was. Card-driven fog-of-war is derived from what a unit can actually accomplish, rather than what the commander thinks it can accomplish.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Very impressive, engrossing card driven tactical wargame, with (looking beyond the cards) some very neat design features. Comparing it to the older wargames in my cupboard, it is most reminiscent of the best bits of Up Front (unpredictability and neat card play) and Ambush! (the events make it feel like a story almost as much as a game, you're rarely sure what the objectives are other than in a broad sense, plus the map artwork has a similar quality). 

Much less deterministic and chess-like than something like Squad Leader. While there's room for both approaches, my instinct leans more towards the CC:E/Ambush!/Up Front way of thinking.
