RATING: 9
I was very skeptical of it at first, but I have to say the game is excellent and I really enjoy playing it.  As others have said, it is a good mix of ASL/SL, Up Front and Memoir '44.  It won't beat ASLSK as a relatively light, combined arms squad-level tactical WWII wargame, but CC:E is magnificient as an infantry-only squad level tactical WWII wargame.

One noteworthy difference in feel with CC:E versus ASL/SL is time scale.  ASL/SL selectively compresses an action that may have taken place over hours into a few minutes, which nicely mitigates its lack of command and control modeling.  On the other hand, CC:E stretches things out such that its seems like playing a very tense, slowed down version of an infantry-only ASL battle.  In this it excels and provides a nice complement to ASL.

RATING: 10
Combat Commander: Europe was my first "real" wargame, and I must say that I was immediately taken with it. The depth of design and attention to detail here is staggering. The rules are robust without being complex. There are many situational considerations, but the gameplay and mechanics are intuitive and smooth. The zoomed-in scale of the game puts you right in the heart of the action (there are chits that represent a single person!). However, it is the scope and timing of decisions and the multiple uses of cards that make this game a work of brilliance. The angst and opportunity costs both players must consider makes for a marvelous dance of tactics, feints, plans, countermeasures, etc. Combat Commander: Europe sweeps you up in its immersive narrative and won't let go until the last shot is fired (or even longer, as you will likely find yourself thinking about the game afterward). This is a truly special game, and it will always have a nostalgic place in my gaming journey.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A couple of years ago I was testing the waters for entry-level wargames, and of course at the time, the most popular options were both tactical, squad-level WWII games, descriptors which didn't mean a whole lot to me. I chose Conflict of Heroes: Awakening the Bear! Russia 1941-1942. I chose wrong.

Combat Commander: Europe seems like the more fluid experience to me. Designer Chad Jensen packs a ton of game into each player's deck of cards, which allow for unit orders, short-term plans, command confusion, random events on the battlefield, combat results, etc. There are no dice in the game, and instead, a full distribution of dice is depicted across a deck of 72 cards. This has some weird but fun effects, as players can start actively distorting their distribution at high levels of play. It also prevents the extreme anomalies that come with dice.

As for the units themselves, CC:E follows a standard template. Units have a movement allowance, firepower rating, and range. Terrain can block line of sight and provide cover (defensive bonuses). Units can initiate melees at close quarters, and combined arms is a key element of dealing damage. Leaders create command zones in which more units can participate in an activation, making it a real problem if they're captured or killed. Etc. It's all incredibly intuitive and easy to follow, though there is a lot to absorb as you go. My opponent commented at one point that the nice thing about the rules is that they're always what you expect them to be. Obviously that depends a little bit on your familiarity with some of the basic concepts here, but generally I'd say he's right.

I can't say whether I'll be playing lots of Combat Commander in the months ahead. I can say that if I'm going to play a tactical squad game, this is it. 

**Update: got well and truly obliterated on my 2nd play. With squad-level games, I feel like I have a tool-set that [i]seems[/i] straightforward enough, but expert combination leaves me feeling like I haven't the first clue how to make anything work.

RATING: 7.5
Based on one (and probably the last) play:  (10-15-09)

One run through of this game, including the sample tutorial in the manual and scenario #1 from the playbook, took nearly six hours.  As we worked through it, I commented to my buddy that I would likely buy a copy of one of the Close Combat games just on the basis of the rules being so well-written and organized. After six hours of CC, I changed my mind rather abruptly - what started out fun turned out to be one of the most frustrating and not-fun games I've ever played.

Around 50% of the cards I drew during the game were unusable (no radios for artillery and no defender for scenario #1), an offense even the simplest gateway game in this genre (Memoir '44) never commits.  Firing mechanics were fiddly and required FAR too much manual consultation.  Weapons feel less like weapons and more like licorice whips meant to flog your opponents with.  The mix of two luck mechanics (dice AND cards) into a supposed wargame lends this game such a random feel that decision-making (other than really obvious things like not running pellmell opportunity fire) seems to be a non-factor.  Meh, meh, meh.

This only gets a two because of the obvious love and care that went into the game, especially the design of the manual.  Otherwise this is NOT a game I'll play again.

Update: (1-10)

Much better this time around.  First scenario is crap and I'd advise against using it unless you have some experience with the game.  Bumping rating up substantially due to having significantly more fun on the second try.

RATING: 8
This is a fun game that reminds me of my days playing Battle Hymn. Although not specifically designed for solitaire as BH was, it plays solo nicely, and the game develops that same "narrative" that pulls the gamer in and personalizes it. The effect is kind of like how it used to feel playing Squad Leader -- you really end up rooting for that one routed squad or the leader who rallies and saves the day. 

There are endless possibilities for play. Many scenarios, none of which play the same twice due to the mechanics. And a random scenario generator as well to build your own, although I am happy to run through all the many scenarios in CCE, CCM, and all the follow-on "Battle Packs" and C3I articles.

Additionally, I am impressed by the loyal following this game has built. Great support on the Geek as well.

RATING: 10
CC:E is an excellent game.  The victory conditions and scenario editor are brilliant.  However, it loses some people because the distribution of cards can leave you unable to fire at a key moment. I play with a few house rules to focus the game better.  First, I allow opportunity fire on your opponent's turn by throwing down your ENTIRE  hand, with each card after the first giving you a +1 to your roll.  Second, I changed the Sniper rules so that the enemy unit nearest to the random hex is suppressed.

I have folio modules 1 through 3 plus the boxed Resistance expansion.

RATING: 6
Initial rating based on solo play.

It seems that the designer has achieved his goal of a fun, chaotic look at WWII tactical gaming.  As many others have stated, chaos is the primary thing going on in this game.  

CC:E repeats and reinforces the stereotypes of the Squad Leader, so it's not much of a historical study.  It seems to lean more toward the oppotunistic "truthy" type of combat systems like Command and Colors (use your cards to get a flag and hope the other guy doesn't have good enough cards to make you pay to much) then the deeper (SL, ASL, ATS, LnL, CoH) games available.  And it takes a bit longer to play than heavier games (like ATS and Lock 'n Load) that include more detail due to the amount of hand churning and random events to resolve.

Still, it's easy to learn WWII tactical.  It's fun to play and the actual mechanics (like fire combat and Line of Sight) are easier than most WWII tactical games.  It's not going to replace ATS for me, but I don't mind playing it on occassion.

RATING: 4.5
My interest was high as it borrows from some favorites, and other than the time-consuming TCS games there is a void for detailed tactical games that place order limits on command and control.  Extremely well-produced game, but the game play disappointed me with every session.  Only during initial setups was I able to think in terms of (as I assume it would be) WWII tactical combat; thereafter, it was a below-average card management game, with progress often determined by random card draw instead of player decisions, and quite tedious.  If the high-luck factor which affected most of our sessions is the norm, then I'd rate it a 2 on the BGG scale.  I hope some consideration is made to improve the basic game play before the sequels are released, but I'm not sure how it can be done...it combines elements of several excellent games, but this soup is already made.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
WWII ground combat (German, US, and Soviet forces) at the squad level. Card driven, big box, 12-scenarios, 12-maps, lots of random events and unlimited replayability. There are lots of expansion and editions planned. Playing time is 1-3 hours for most scenarios.

The game is chaotic as players have limited control over what happens. Some people don't like CC:E because of its chaotic nature. My response to them is that if you think combat is an orderly, by-the-numbers process, you have been spoiled by earlier, non-historical wargames. Try reading S.L.A. Marshall's "Night Drop, the American Airborne Drop of Normandy" and you will see how chaotic it is.

RATING: 9
Small unit combat is chaotic and random.  CC:E represents this aspect of warfare better than any other game on the subject.  And it's just plain fun to play!  I think the level of complexity is just right, hitting that sweet spot between too simple and mind-numbingly detailed.  I like the uncertainty and chaos introduced by the card-driven mechanic.  I think this kind of uncertainty and inability to control your units in a "god-like" way represents, better than any other game on the subject, the chaotic nature of combat.

I hope the designer and developers leave "well enough alone" and don't start layering on additional levels of unnecessary chrome and complexity for the sake of "realism".  Many games have died from "expansion bloat" (remember how good a game Star Fleet Battles was before the expansions?).  I hope this doesn't happen with Combat Commander.  That said, I'm certainly looking forward to additional expansions and the new nationalities that will be introduced.  Expansion #3 will be the Pacific.  I think that will be a very interesting expansion.(December 2006)

RATING: 9.5
This is my favorite war game so far. Not being a huge war-gamer this is the one that has convinced me to play more war-games. Its rules whilst seemingly endless are easily interpreted once you've had a game under your belt.
Very tactical and an emotional roller coaster. Expect to be on a high as everything seems to go your way only to feel a crushing loss as a couple of rolls or events change the course of war. Very realistic. Here is an example. I had artillery & over 3 rounds I pounded the area with smoke grenades, there was a mass of smoke covering all the open ground, the Germans were in a building waiting, I moved large quantities of troops up close to the building under the cover of smoke, there was nothing he could do. I turn an event card and got Breeze, all the smoke blew away, I had about 8 units standing in the open waving merrily at the German machine guns. RUN AWAY!
Love it.

RATING: 4

Too much card luck, and don't give me that chaos of war justification. Can you really advance through open terrain at a machinegun nest or bunker and not get shot at? Try it! Damned card games!

One last point. The games that I have won were just an exercise in dumping cards as fast as possible once I had a point lead (and that's easy if you are the defender). All I did was get rid of all the cards I could every turn and triggered the end of the game to win. What kind of example is this of small unit combat? Now where is this in the small unit combat handbook? Seriously, I stared at the cards more than the map!

RATING: 8
I like this game! The cards are a great mechanic, and there is no CRT or complicated sequence of play. Just jump right in and have fun.

There are some very gamey aspects to CC:E, but then it is a game. Last time I played, I held a VP building with my last unit, and so I was ahead in points. My opponent had more units, but was struggling to find the right cards to get everyone organized. So, we waited and discarded for maybe 15 consecutive turns. Obviously, the time length of each turn is abstract and not rigid, but it really pulls you out of the action, and you're left realizing you're just playing a hand-management sim.

Just know, this is not the solution to the "total control" issue of ASL. I'd argue it was never an issue to begin with, but hey.

Take all of this with a grain of salt. I think CC:E is a lot of fun to play, and I've taught it to a few of my friends. I like!

RATING: 6
Yeah, it has a lot of randomness, but personally I feel that imperfect control over your units (wanting your guys to move forward, but not having a Move card) and imperfect information (which manifests itself through Sniper triggers etc.) are more realistic than certain other tactical games where you have perfect information and perfect control over (and coordination between) all of your units.

With more plays, I may get tired of what feels like really "swingy" luck, but so far I'm still enjoying it, and after a game in which you get hammered, you can identify specific mistakes you made, or specific events which went the wrong way for you (like laying smoke between your mortar team and an enemy machinegun team, and having the wind blow it away at just the wrong time).

RATING: N/A
Preliminary thoughts:

Counters are punched and rules have been read. The system is simpler than I anticipated (not a bad thing) which means it should eventually see some playing time. Overall I like what i've read and seen. I just need to play it.

I would say this game (rulewise) appears to be simpler than Panzer Grenadier (though interactions between various order and action cards could complicate things) and Tide of Iron. Tide of Iron's rulebook is wordy, and I found Combat Commander's to be more friendly than both PG and ToI. This may be because of my familiarity with tactical WW2 games, however, as they're all pretty similar at their core (LOS, LOF, terrain, cover, movement points, actions, etc).

Component quality is top notch, and I love the oversized hexes. No more huge counter stacks is a definite plus.

After finally playing I can say that this is one of the best rulebooks i've ever had the pleasure to use.

Definitely a fun game. 

What I like about CC:E is how things like your discard limit are used to represent tactical flexibility. Again postures (attack/defend/recon) being represented by how many cards you can hold is another great mechanic (CC:P looks to really expand on this) - it's just a great way to add chrome to a game without adding cumbersome rules.

RATING: 9
Pure genius! I love this game. Great tactical simulation covering so many types of scenarios. Perhaps the best gameplay of any tactical combat game, (but no tanks, so not up to the Yaquinto classics I rate higher). The supremely elegant solution to the "god's view" of the battlefield, that of using cards to activate, is not only the most innovative idea in decades, but implemented flawlessly. Further, the ease of play to achieve these results is just gangbusters! Once you get your head around the distinctions between "cover" and "hindrance", and the funky op fire rules, the rest of the game plays like buttah! My new favorite.

RATING: 6
They say it is ASL Up Front CDG.  And so they say.  The cards, rulebook and playbook are very nicely done. The box cover and maps are not.  They are a bit cartoonish to my taste.  But I like very much the idea of random scenario generation system, with the point value system. This should be done with every tactical wargame for players to enjoy. I haven't heard a lot about ASL doing the same. It is not anything new which has been so popular in PC game system for a long time, from Steel Pathers to Civilization. 

The thick box comes with stuffs which do not fully fill out. So there is a lot of space inside. Don't be fooled by its apperance. The dice results are printed on the cards.  And the system does not shuffle the cards everytime a player draws.  So when you draw a single card for each combat, chances are that the probabilities change after each draw.  Deterministic on the apperance by your card deck and I wonder if this could replace the rolling of two dices altogether, which feels certainly better.  Yet the events, hex location for some of the events take place are randomnly determined.  It is this kind nice twist shown all throughout the game.  

Which scenario is the best to start? I prefer German and Russian.  We played scenario 10 "Command School" and enjoyed it a lot. An invaluable substitute to those who don't like the simpler Memoir'44.

Yep, this is a good base game with short playing time. Its random scenario generator offers tremendous playability value. I didn't like it the first time when I didn't have a clue of what was I doing. However, when I played the expansion game on Stalingrad, things starting to become clear. However, I think I have enough with the system as I didn't buy further the other expansions/ battle packs on the western front, e.g. Pacific, Mediterranean, Paratroopers, Normandy. (Note in 2014 Dec: finally I got all the Battle Packs except Nr. 1 Paratroopers and all the boxes except the Pacific.) Perhaps mostly because I thought it was too luck-dependent sometimes and the system might only be good under specific circumstances only, those that I defined to be the high-activity-intensity tactical situation. For protracted or long maneuvering war at tactical level, this game shows its limitations.

some great stories come out of this one.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
The game is very random and it's hard to plan a strategy (like chess) because of it.  However, you will enjoy the game a lot more if you buy into the thought that war is random and the best plans can fall to goo even on sunny days.

Because of the 100% card based system you can't do the basics like move, shoot or attack without a card to support it.  Your squad can stand out in the open for multiple turns and be fired on.  The side note here is to make sure you have all the cards in your hand before you move.  If you don't you could be stalled out in the open unable to move or shoot.

Another harsh part of the game is that hand to hand kills instantly (with one card) but fire teams with huge fire power can never kill anything with a single round of fire.  Knowing this you have to split up your firing so some of the team fires and the second part of the team fires.  I still feel though if you have one massive fire attack that you should be able to KIA a unit (it's just not how the game works is all).

Other odd things are that your team can be routed and have to retreat for no reason other than the other player had the Rout card.  In one of the games my guys got run off the map and the other player didn't have to fire a shot other than the first one that broke the team.  Again you will have to use some imagination here.  If your guys retreated from the fight they simple have had enough and ran away.

The game designer probably had a good time randomizing all the orders (move, shoot, assault,...) actions (order modiers) and events.  I like the random actions and events.  For me I like to move, shoot, assault and etc ~ everything else can be random)

In the realm of card driven games this is top in it's class.  If you want something more like ASL or chess then you not like it as much.

RATING: 9
Wow this game is just great. My first game of it was such a frustrating experience, I felt like a piece of flotsam and jetsam just being pushed around by the fog of war unable to effectively achieve anything. I would command my guys to make a dash for the trees under the cover of smoke, only to find them freezing on the spot, suppressed under fire in open ground. Then for the wind to pick up and blow the smoke away revealing them like a burnt sunday roast from a smokey oven.

I was getting frustrated and hated the game. Even my German opponent consoled me! Things were bad. Then I read the instructions ... and something clicked. Maybe it was the section in the manual that explicitly stated that euro-gamers would have the reaction I had. That they needed consoling and nudging through the chaos of the game.

I came out the other side a different gamer. I love the chaos that this game stirs up. My fear of chits with endless numbers on them has been quelled (well at least enough that I enjoy this game, I'm not ready to start playing games with tweezers yet!). I finally get that this is just a card game and taking the time to cycle through your cards is not always a bad thing. Your opponent is having just as hard a time as you.

If you haven't tried this before and you are in any way interested, try it. Just be ready to be consoled by the enemy as they pop their head up from a bunker and take pot shots at you ... because most of the time that's all they are pot shots ... grab your rifle, gather your squad and run like the wind for the trees, you might just make it.

UPDATE

Wow. Every game of this is different and Combat Commander for me seems to have a logarithmic enjoyment scale. My first few games were a struggle but since then (and my understanding that both sides struggle with cards in hand and no-one can do everything exactly when they want to) the enjoyment factor has just shot through the roof to the point where this is becoming one of my favourite games.

This time we played a scenario from the sea lion pack (which my friend has and is now on my radar!) where the Brits were holding out in a 3 story church tower and private Peavsly (name from memory I know it's wrong) sat at the top of it with a rifle which seemed to have the accuracy of some sort of 22nd century technology that was picking off my German paratroopers as they stormed his location.

The story that unfolded was fantastic with the Germans eventually taking the clock tower and then trying to hold out as the Brits stormed it. Throw in a wild roaming hero, some constantly extremely poor ordinance by the Germans and then a freakish perfect hit to wipe out a Brit leader who was co-ordinating the British storming of an outlying building that would have brought on a British win from a German surrender and you have just a brilliant game.

I was not one for this sort of game, or so I thought!

RATING: 9
VERY tentative rating after 1 game.  Unsure of how the cards would cause the game to play, I came away [i]very[/i] impressed.  I can't wait to play again.

Update 12/28/06: Raised half a point to 8.5 after two more games.  Incredibly fun and fast, and incredibly dynamic.  You're never out until the game ends.  Gives a good feel of what I imagine the chaos on the ground could feel like.

Update 12/31/06: Raised another half point to 9.  I can't get enough of this game.  After a couple of plays, the flow of the game is nice and quick and the action and tension is always there.  At the moment, this is a game I keep wanting to play.

Including this one, I've only played two squad-level WWII games, the other being [b]Squad Leader[/b].  These two games inhabit different game spaces.  SL is the heavier game; it's more methodical and allows players to come up with and enact a plan.  CC is more chaotic; it attempts to show the chaos on a battlefield at the low level.  I haven't found this to be a hinderence; indeed, planning with what you have and for opportunities is incredible in this game.  Also, as it's card-driven, it's fast; there aren't a bunch of steps you may or must do each turn.  Your orders and actions (as well as random events) are all determined by cards, so the pacing of the game matches the chaos.  All in all, it's one heck of a fun, addictive game that produces an exciting, believable narrative.  I can't wait to play again.

RATING: 10
A great game that has a lot of small annoying features. 

Sniper! trigger is just too random (determine random hex and break one unit in or adjacent to that hex). I think it should be implemented like the Suppressing fire event (suppress one unit in the LOS of a friendly MG) that rewards good positioning of a machine gun. According to the rulebook the Sniper! trigger represents things such as friendly fire, panic or dirt in the eye. Ok, it may be realistic to model such things but it is not very rewarding when your opponent gets some dirt in his eye and dies compared to when you cut him in half with your machine gun.

Air support event: people die in a random hex. Lame.

Élan event: increase your surrender level by one. The scenarios in the playbook tend to drag too long. This makes them even longer.

Battlefield Integrity: Victory points for each of your opponents eliminated units. When you are behind this does almost nothing. When in lead you get further away. Bad.

I gave this full 10. Despite of these annoying features it is a very fun game. The card driven order/action system works. Playing each of the three nationalities is very distinct. Rulebook is great. Maps look good.

RATING: 8.5
I'm drawn to this game in a wierd way.  There's a few things I really don't like about it, yet so much that I love about it.

The components are great, from the awesome maps to the huge variety of infantry units and weapons.  It's easy to learn, and easy to play, with some nice safeguards against lousy swings of luck.  The game communicates nicely the tension and drama of warfare, as the action tends to happen in short bursts followed by periods of anticipation.

However, something about the makeup of the decks that determine your actions in the game leads to long stretches of fishing for specific cards just to get your squad to run accross a road, or shoot at a stationary target right in the open.  It makes for a kind of choppy and overly long game.  It's just short of excellence, which is really a shame.

RATING: 10
Combat Commander: Europe is one of my absolute favorite games. It is not only fun but cards combined with game play make for a great story.

Is it the most realistic treatment of WWII. Perhaps not. But I don't care--even if I am a professional historian who has dabbled in military history -- in both publications and as a fellow at West Point. I can play this with my pre-teen son and have a blast---quite a bit of complexity without hurting the brain. The introductory game is a great way to learn the game---it is basically a walk-through of the game with a "stacked" deck. One walkthrough game and we were ready to go.

Rarely do the games last for more than 2 hours but the yelling and screaming that can go on during the game makes it seem like far less. Once you get the hang of it, the game moves quickly.

The lack of vehicles don't bother me in the least...again, perhaps because my primary concern isn't simulation.

That said, some critiques decry the lack of control because of cards...being unable to move, etc. Or the chaos and wild swings of fortune that can occur. All I can say is that having ready accounts of military history from many different eras, the one constant on a battlefield is chaos and irrationality. War is not always logical or clear with perfect information and intelligence....something gamers don't often remember.

Without a doubt, this game has theme and narrative more than any other wargame that I've ever played! I can't recommend it enough.

RATING: 7
This was one of those impulse purchases and I'm looking forward to playing it. The thing is that I tend to learn rules by reading them in bed at night so that they can soak in as I sleep. I then read them again before giving the game a go, and I find this helps. The problem with CC:E is, I can't stay awake long enough to absorb any of the rules. I've been trying for a week now. I guess I'll just have to go through this one when I'm wide awake and run a mock skirmish.

Another point: though I think the cards are great - very high quality with clear and appropriate artwork - I can't understand why people think the counters are of such high quality. I think they're thin, flimsy and prone to tear, plus the supplied counter tray isn't nearly adequate to carry them. The die-cutting isn't fantastic, and the tiles needed a lot of tidying and clipping.

I know I don't have much experience of owning hex-and-counter games, but if this is what a high-quality example is like, I won't be buying many more. Call me a chrome-whore, but I like bits that feel good, and that I have confidence will last.

Yes, gameplay's the thing, but I'm shallow when it comes to bits.

[Update:] Wow. This is a real "Shit Happens" game. I haven't, to date, played anything that's quite so chaotic, where picking up a new card from the deck gives you an itch of anticipation in your fingertips. So much so that I don't think I've really understood it, so I've loaned it to someone who is much more into rulebook absorption than I am.

That said, as a first solo impression I really like it but, other than variances in the Fate Decks, I don't know what CC:P or the other expansions can offer. This, plus the fact that I'm not thrilled by the components in what is an extremely-expensive-for-what-you-get game means I probably won't play, or even look at, the others. My main reason is that I might be tempted, but I can't justify the expense on this one, nor the time to read their rule variants. CC:E will have to be enough.

[Update:] This game takes a while to play - 3 hours each for the first two scenarios.

Having had a few goes through now, I do enjoy the game; however, I wonder whether its thrill is sustainable - after all, there's so much about it that's luck. Sometimes, you just don't get the cards you need. Last game, because of a series of TIME events meaning I had to shuffle like it was Dominion, I went six turns without a Recover order being available. This meant that my opponent was beating the shit out of me with attacks and Rout orders and I couldn't rally.

Whilst I prefer to win games, I'm not a sore loser, but it does frustrate me when the game gets in the way of any player. I don't mind games where dice are rolled or "you play with the cards you're dealt", but this was out of control. I'll write that one off to experience - sometimes things just don't go your way - but will be monitoring the game to see if this is recurrent. I think there's too much luck in here though for it ever to be a real favourite. It's good, but not exceptional.

RATING: 8
Finally bought into this system...I think it's pretty clever, it's a war movie tactical game more than some hardcore sim of tactical combat, but that's fine. Very interactive, with 'interrupt' type card actions.

Pretty obviously inspired to some degree by both Squad Leader and Up Front. I love the unique maps (rather than the geomorphic maps in so many other tactical games), and the large hexes, and the low piece density. The small map footprint is also appealing. Takes perhaps slightly longer to play than you'd expect? Not certain.

One thing I love about this system is the sort of random victory markers. I think it would have been amazing if the original Squad Leader had included something like that. That, and the really clever random scenario generator are highlights. Not the be all end all tactical system, but deservedly popular, I'd say. 

Also, very well written, tight rules, which carries weight with me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I'd have to play it more to know exactly how much I like it, but I'm quite sure I do like it. However, I think I like it much more as a game than a military simulation or exercise.

I'm a fan of dice, so I was somewhat put off by the dice-on-card idea. I'm not sure I unreservedly endorse the idea. But it seems to work well. The deck is also a resource, a timer and a random event generator.

I'm not yet sure how much luck is a factor. Certainly it is one, because you can be completely hosed by your card draw. I suppose if you're looking for a different sort of military exercise--you have perfect information and can just explore tactics without worrying about fog of war--then this won't work for you. On the other hand, if you're looking to explore chaos--men in position can't move, mission objectives are murky, random events interrupt play--then this will work for you.

One issue I have is that--at least at this scale--the assumption should be that a small unit will be able to move, shoot, etc. most of the time. Units seem to spend a lot of time doing nothing (waiting for orders?) when they should know what to do already. And you shouldn't need to call headquarters to get the okay to shoot at someone close assaulting your position. This game seems to want to make everything unpredictable, when certain things should be much more unpredictable than others. This isn't necessarily a flaw, just a design choice designed to create a certain effect. It works, but it seems less than ideal to me.

The best strategy for the defender can often be to just churn through cards as fast as possible to get Time events. It's gamey and detracts a bit from the immersion that I enjoy.

I'm also not enamored of the close assault mechanic, especially eliminating surviving units that exceed stacking limits. It seems like they (or at least some) should be able to retreat back to their original hex or be suppressed in their original hex or something. Maybe I've just been hosed one too many times by my opponent always having multiple ambush cards!

Complaints notwithstanding, this is still a [i]fun [/i]game, and that's really the most important thing. It does what it sets out to do, and it's a very clean, attractive, functional package that will provide many, many hours of fun for the WWII gamer. I just ignore the flaws (mostly.) They bump the rating down a bit, but it's still fun. It's just not massively fun.

I think I would enjoy playing it semi-regularly. I don't think this type of game could become my favorite, but it would be worth playing it now and again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Odd tactical WW2 game. 

Up Front works (due to the level of abstraction), but the map added to CC just makes the action depicted seem weird and very gamey... units under fire unable to move into cover because they lack a Move card, squads not firing at units advancing right at them across several hundred feet of open ground because they don't have a Fire card and so on.

Not that other tactical WW2 games are more "realistic" (ok, but just about all tactical games at this scale are much more game than simulation), but the narrative in a CC game is very often surreal.  Frustrating to spend several turns discarding tons of cards because you can't do anything with your current hand.  

Very difficult to execute standard fire/suppress/maneuver  (the Four F's) squad level tactics due to the difficulty in building the right card combos in your hand.  Often, even massive amounts of fire directed at a single hex is relatively ineffective... units can break and then recover fairly easily without any losses.  I don't like using the card deck as a random number generator instead of a dice roll.

Wished that the design used cards to provide random events and even combat resolution while using somewhat standard wargame mechanics for fire and movement, but alas, that was not the design that we have here.

The game in many ways, seems to almost play itself.

If you want a game about WW2 small unit tactics, best look elsewhere.  If you want a good game, play something else.

RATING: 10
Combat Commander as a game system is a stunning achievement. I'm astounded at how it confounds and exceeds my expectations with every play. You might not see it until you've played enough for the rules overhead to mostly disappear, but this game is rich with narrative in a way that some so-called thematic games could only hope to achieve. Part squad-level tactical combat game, part experience game, the key to enjoying this one is to embrace the chaos and just watch the story develop.

Lastly, this game has the honor of what I consider to be the best rulebook ever.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

CC is a card driven wargame with lots of random mayhem to cope with whilst trying to achieve game objectives. Each game played is so narratively strong that I can remember details from games played months ago. An absolute gem!

RATING: 8
This is coming from a person who owns only a couple of games that can be called "wargames," and no GMT games other than Twilight Struggle.  And I've never played ASL.

CC:E is certainly not simple, and the rules can, at times, be a bit arcane.  But if you're willing to put up with a learning curve it seems this is a pretty good squad-level game.  The hand management really gives a rhythm to the game that I enjoy.  You have to choose carefully what you will do with the cards in hand, as only 3-4 orders can be given in a turn, and then only if you have the right cards.  Lots of neat mechanics make this what I would call an "elegant" game - that is, there are many things that can be represented with a finite set of rules.  It's amazing how many purposes the deck of cards has - die rolls, random events, counterattacks, modifiers to die rolls, random hex position, artillery scatter, and of course, an order.  I'll play again, and I might even buy it.

RATING: 7
A fun infantry squad wargame. It isn't fast by eurogame standards, especially because of frequent rule referral unless the players are very familiar with the rules. However, the card system is a great idea and adds a lot of chaos and and keeps both players engaged even when it isn't their turn, which makes the game feel fast.

The rulebook is OK. Despite what others have said, I think the rulebook could have been laid out differently for the unitiated to the usual GMT style, which appears to assume quite a bit of knowledge about card-driven games. The rules make for a fun game but aren't intuitive and have quite a few exceptions to consider and quite a few rules are easily forgotten (eg sniper checks on broken weapons). Even after 3 games under my belt I still find myself referring to the rules frequently, which I suppose will ease after a few more games.

RATING: 8
Combat Commander is fun to play.  However...
  
After wasting several turns discarding worthless cards, I'm finally able to fire on the Germans in a victory point building.  My leader activates three hexes and I decide to fire each squad seperatly.  I fire and the Germans are broken.  Great!  I fire again and the Germans generate a hero.  Ok, no problem.  I fire at them a third time and the squad goes from regular to veteran status!  The Germans went from broken to invinceable uber-unit in an instant!  What's going on here?  Additional fire on their hex actually helped them!  The Germans easily rallied the next turn and fired right back using "spray fire" which broke every one of my units.  I was not able to rally them for the rest of the game because I didn't have the right card.   Apparently my commander decided it was nap time.  In fact, I was unable to draw the cards I needed to do anything purposeful. My opponent could have walked right up to my units and bitch-slapped them and I wouldn't have the cards to react.  Meanwhile, one of my squads far removed from the action decides to become supressed thanks to one of many random events.  

Yes, I understand that combat is chaotic and planning goes out the window when the lead starts flying.  However, I think CC is unrealistically chaotic, gamey, random, and luck driven to the point of being absurd. I like how snipers pop up, but other things seem to appear like magic. 

Update: Years after my introduction to CC:E, I played it again and, knowing what to expect, I had a blast.  I look forward to many more games and my rating has been revised.

RATING: 4
Wanted very much to like CC:E as I'm a fan of chaos mechanisms in wargames. CC:E can be wildly arbitrary, which is fine for some I guess. In playing two consecutive times with the same opponent the box handed the victory to one of us--where's the satisfaction in that? Our feeling was, "Oh, I won[or lost]? Great... There goes two and a half hours of effort."

The problem isn't randomness; it's the degree of randomness. 

CC:E is very provocative however. Reviewing it caused me to re-evaluate what I want to see in a wargame. (I don't think this is an easy question for most gamers to answer, actually). For me, as far as tactical games are concerned, I want to learn something about actual tactics. Postgame sessions of a tactical should be revelatory of mistakes and the reasoning behind calculated risks. Yeah, yeah it's just a game--but some games lead to more intelligent or profound after action reviews than this one, rather than escalating rationalizations for bizarre occurrences. 

Why are there tactical doctrines in the first place? Because weird stuff happens. But CC:E doesn't teach anything other than "combat is random." A good tactician knows this but also knows that combat is less random than you might think.

The price is also too high. Hopefully I can trade it off before CoH occupies this niche.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I love the card-driven wargame system, and while Combat Commander: Europe is not a card-driven game in that strict sense, it uses its cards in such wonderful ways that it captures much of the feel and the excitement of a CDG.  This is a game in which the cards are everything.  They are your dice, your actions, your orders, your events, and your randomizer.  And the card management aspect that this introduces is truly fantastic.  The game is deep but streamlined, and it plays very well and rather quickly.  I love that it has eschewed the traditional UN symbols on the chits for more graphically pleasing representations of the soldiers, and everything about this game is well made.

The game can be frustrating if the cards are preventing you from what you want to do, such as not giving you move cards when you want to move, or fire cards when you want to fire.  This will definitely bother some people, but I actually enjoy the choices that it forces you to make.  This is definitely one of those "make the best of what you have" games, and some people will be turned off by that.  But if you enjoy maximizing your sometimes limited resources, you will love this.  A fantastic game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Met the designer (He is an awesome guy! his wife rocks too!)

I would consider purchasing this because of the quality of the graphics and components. The personality and attitude of the designer. The reputation of the publisher.

I would not purchase this game because in my opinion:
It is a CDG and *I* don't believe that there is any way you can truly build a winning strategy for a specific situation as you will always be subject to the luck of the draw for movement as well as for combat. In dice driven Squad/Platoon level games, the dice don't control movement, so you can build a strategy that you have control over when it comes to achieving your objectives. True you have no control over the combat results or the morale of your troops, but that is more historical to me than anything that uses cards.
2: It is another ASL/Advanced Tobruk/Panzer Grenadier/Lock and Load/ ASL Starter Kit/ Panzer Leader/Panzer Blitz style game; and while imitation is the highest form of flattery, do we really need another?

If CCE were a tool in your arsenal to introduce more gamers to the hobby because of it's easy learning curve then I would expect it to be rated 10 across the board. However, for grognards (war gamers not Euros)when the available gaming dollars we have to spend is in most cases limited, can we afford to use them to purchase a system that is a very close relative to something we most likely already own?

If you are not a platoon/squad level gamer then this is definitely one of the two games I would choose as a vehicle to get into that level of gaming.

RATING: 9
This is a fun, fairly simple, game on WWII infantry combat. The rules are easy to understand leaving the player to concentrate on what's important - the current, ever-changing, tactical situation and how to manipulate it to win the scenario. The game has a very nice narrative flow where the pendulum of fortune can rapidly swing from one side to the other normally leaving the result in question up until the final moment. The command control limits and the randomness will mean this game will not appeal to everyone but the game scratches my WWII tactical itch with five percent of the time investment that I'd need to learn ASL and covering eighty percent of what I want to play. It's not ASL and it's not trying to be.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I shied away from this initially because of the lack of armour, but when I finally chose it over Tide of Iron, I found that the lack of armour wasn't important - it's a brilliant game anyway. The use of the cards for multiple purposes (orders, actions, events dice-rolls etc) adds to replayablity, though the frustrations of not having the right combination of cards in you hand is constant!

You can't do everything you want on every turn, so you have to prioritise and hope you get the results you need to move on to your next priority.  However, even shots that seem certain to break or kill a unit can be influenced by your opponent playing an action card in your turn. When the game ends is never set in stone either, and leads to many cinematic "just one more push (turn)" type actions, when you a praying for the game to go on so as you can get that elusive victory point to win, whilst you opponent is praying for the game to end for the same reason. This variable time encourages the attacker to take the initiative, as he is bound to lose if he doesn't, because the defender usually starts off holding most of the victory points.

National characteristics are also built into the system without seeming overbearing, with the year the scenario is set in dictating troop quality and equipment.

Anyway, I can't praise this game enough, and the criticisms it received for being too random don't seem fair. Things go wrong in battles despite the best-laid plans (guns jam, people run away at the wrong moment), but if you use the wrong tactics, like advancing over open ground towards a HMG, then you will lose (opportunity fire can be punishing). I read somewhere that the 1st play of this for someone was like playing the original Squad Leader for the 1st time, and that struck a chord with me - it has that freshness which I remember from Squad Leader, and that is definitely no bad thing. 

Recommended (especially if, like me, you are dithering over buying it!)

Haters are gonna hate. Gamers are gonna game.

RATING: 3.5
Interesting system with vast replay potential as the card play should keep the same scenario from ever really playing out the same.  

Small arms fire seems on the weak side as there is no way to kill a squad outright and most fire attacks seem to have no result (large firegroups are necessary to stand a chance at impacting enemy units through firepower).  Contrast that to melee which has a very simple system to the immediate elim of a squad.

While the cards bring the most fun to the game, they can also bring the most pain - card fatugue.  Be prepared for going long spells where you do very little that you want to due to poor card hands (or you could do something - just not nearly what you want to do!).

RATING: 8
After playing this once and getting burned (my unit got booted from my key objective by a random fire...) I thought more about it over the last year or so and decided that I like the concept still, even if the implementation left me wanting.  I must say that it was more fun solitaire than I expected, and I think it's because it's easier to just look at like a series of events that you're watching play out.  I've raised my rating from it's initial 6 since I realize that I was going into it with an incorrect understanding of what my role as the commander was.  I think I was just too used to games where I could actually plan my turns out a few in advance better.  Looking at it more like a commander simply trying to manage battlefield chaos (Chaos Commander anyone?) to get my objectives completed and realizing the the events that get triggered shouldn't be looked at with any sort of causal relationship to the die rolls taking place make it quite a bit more fun.

RATING: 4
Combat Commander is an exercise in chaos gaming in which the chaos seems overwhelming. It certainly didn't feel anything like World War 2 combat. One imagines a real infantry battle as having an impetus that is ground down, or suddenly peters out into inertia. In CC it was difficult to get any impetus going at all. It was surprising how the game seemed to drag. For long periods of play, I felt unable to do much and discarded hand after worthless hand. Play was fitful and spasmodic. Doing anything 'tactical' invariably required multiple cards or card combos to pull off, and these combinations were rarely available. When things did rolling (if only briefly), then combat turned out to be very indecisive. Units in cover were tough to break and there always seemed to be sufficient recovery cards on hand to negate the effects of fire. Overall, this was a game too chaotic to exert any coherent control over. The lack of options made it dreary and uninvolving. The clear heritage, the descent from Up Front and SL/ASL, invites invidious comparisons. Colour me underwhelmed. The counters are pretty, though.

RATING: 10
Best design ever played

RATING: 10
I rated the game after 179 FtF plays and 121 solo plays, Yep, it does make three hundred.

I only thank the heavens that 30 years ago the current  philosophy of gaming just could not come out with a game system like CC. At the time a sort of "modernist" thought was dominating that wanted games to "simulate" everything, but just could not understand that the battlefield is fuzzy, and chaos is what they had to cope with. CC is a true postmodern game, that gets rid of the neat algorithms of the true battlefield and plunge players into the chaos of the real battlefield.

CC is so addicting, were it on the market 30 years ago, what would have become of my studies?

RATING: 6
Combat Commander uses cards to acquire orders so you can do something in the game.  A unique concept but I found it doesn't really work that well. 

The designers say this game is not based on reality as much as it is based on play ability. That is very true.  Lots of things in the game end up making no sense as to why they are happening.  So that is just bizarre for a war game. I play a lot of games using cards and non are as strange as this one.

I also found it very frustrating in that I didn't get an order that any normal combat situation would dictate that it could happen.  But I can't make it happen because the cards didn't come up to be able to do the obvious normal order that one would do.  

The game felt like a card game of limited actions wrapped with the disguise of a combat game with a lot of frustration and down right stupid things coming up.  

A 6 is a generous evaluation based mostly on art work, well written rules, and a lot of additions to the game.

RATING: 10
Hands down my favorite game and series. Will play any time, with one of the hundreds of GMT scenarios or using the outstanding scenario builder.

The card driven format creates the perfect fog-of-war and makes a great narrative. CC is an easy 10, and I've actually had to recalibrate the ratings for almost everything else in my collection. Own several squad-level WWII games, but CC is the one I keep coming back for. Recommend this title to anyone interested in tactical games.

Conclusion: las estrategias no son validas mas alla de un par de turnos y preparate a pasar de la sonrisa a la tristeza varias veces a lo largo de una partida.

RATING: 10
Hex and counters tactical combat game with Word War II in Europe as its theme, and squad-level in scale. The production quality is good for its genre, with lots of units (with good artwork), twelve scenarios and six double-sided maps. The orders, actions, events and rolls are all made through "fate" decks specific for each army (American, German and Russian). The random events that can occur in each game and its random scenario generator add to the fun and replayability. It's also rules-light by wargame standards, specially because it's card-driven, which also greatly reduces analysis-paralysis. There's (little) room for improvement, but there's yet to appear a better squad-level wargame.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
CC resolves mostly around hand management, going through the deck and trying to create the hand you want. The gameplay just becomes overly procedural since you do the same things over and over again the same way each turn.

Historical accuracy is blown away with some of the very static game system elements and on the other hand very random occurances for each scenario. Running off the map to get victory points? There are just many possibilities for overly gamey gameplay that makes absolute no sense historically. 

Recovering units is way too easy. The best you can usually do with ranged attack is to break one or two enemy units. Your opponent will probably recover those units next round and effectively making your last turn "no effect". All that just makes a longer game with no extra contribution to the experience, quite the opposite. On the other hand melee is overly powerful and building up a hand of advance and ambush cards is the only way to cause major casualties in a shorter time frame. 

The exchange of fire could go on for hours since the game doesn't have a fixed number of rounds. The unfixed timer doesn't make the game any more historical, it just makes the game last longer which is not reasonable compared to the lighness of the game. If you add up the time for the setup and full game, the total session time probably averages at 3-4 hours or even more. Over 2 hours is just way too long for the lightness and the same way repeating player turns. This game shouldn't last more than an hour. You'll just do the same over and over again for way too many times.When you add up random snipers and events that don't necessarily make any sense, then you'll have something that doesn't make an enjoyable experience.

The game creates a random variation of a given scenario each time but when you add up some of the gamey, unhistorical and gameplaywise annoying elements then you don't have an accurate historical representation at any level. For example the Tolvajärvi scenario from the Mediterranean: The Russian convoy is ambushed in the first round but yet the Finnish player might have a poor hand and you can't even shoot at them. The Russian can just melee rush from the road to the woods. Really? Basically any Russian would be blown away with Finnish rifle fire. The Russians attack from the road and ambush the Finnish soldiers in the woods; it doesn't make any sense whatsoever.

If a game represents history at any level at all in a given scenario, it really needs to have special rules for each scenario that modify the basic rules or add up special rules and that they make the scenario somewhat historical representation. Many wargames handle this very well but CC fails at this utterly. 

Overall I don't see CC gameplaywise enjoyable experience. It also fails as a historical representation. When you add up the overly long but yet very light, random and yet same way sequencing gameplay then I'm fully confident to say that CC is a game that I won't be playing in the future.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A two player wargame for world war 2 on a very small scale (counters represent single officers, weapons or small squads). There are no vehicles, this represents small unit infantry engagements. The game is quite traditional but instead of a very lengthy rule book the players each have a deck of cards which gives the basic optins (Move, fire, Artillery, Recover, Advance (for close combat) and also brings in most of the exceptions through events and actions (representing snipers, hand grenades, automatic fire etc). 
It has an interesting pacing (lots of pauses and halts punctuated by dramatic unfolding action) which certainly feels like accounts you read of infantry engagements. Its biggest problem is that is makes no real concession to the non-wargamer so it it is still a minority product and the book includes no solo rules - which is something you really need with a wargame that has a lengthy play time.

RATING: 10
My rating is based on one play.  In a nutshell, the game is a card driven Squad Leader lite.  There are so many elements of Squad Leader in this game (including the mapsheets which look like they could be color photocopies of the SL mapboards - which makes the claim in the rules that the mapsheet art was done by the designer seem a bit strange) that comparison with that game is inevitable. But it has so little in common with Up Front that I really can't understand how some of the comments here are making that comparison.  Anyway, forgetting about the other games, CC Europe was rather fun to play despite some lengthy bouts of discarding cards in an attempt to find something needed or even usable.  I'm giving the game the benefit of the doubt that in subsequent scenarios (we played the first one), the availability of more ordinance will make more cards usable, and therefore less need for seemingly endless discards.  The Russian player had a heck of a time inflicting any damage on the Germans who were in buildings or even woods.  But with only one play, it would be unfair to assume balance flaws.  Anyway, I like card games, card driven games, and wargames.  This doesn't really qualify for the latter, but it's interesting enough as a card driven game that I'll definitely be giving it some more play.

After many, many plays, I've upgraded my rating to 10. The game is now my favorite game of all time.

RATING: 8
Rating pending a second play, at minimum an "8", but I was postively stricken by the seamless cardplay, the player agency, and the ablity of the game to tell such a compelling battlefield narrative.
***
Update: After another play, my sentiment is unchanged. For context, this is (so far) the only hex-and-counter war game I have played. It can be difficult to appreciate this game from just appearances, but what it lacks in visual appeal, it more than makes up for in rich narrative; providing battlefield drama that is, at times, almost cinematic in nature. Some gems from our first scenario play: Russian troops hidden in a nearby woods withstood sustained gunfire over the course of a few minutes, surging into an objective post and overwhelming the German troops within. German troops advancing strategically upon a Russian position, only to have three of their squads break due to a hidden sniper's attack. Half of the broken troops were then immediately routed off the battlefield. A final encounter between the two opposing sides culminated in a brutal melee by lakeside, leaving no survivors. 

I acknowledge that treating these events as such requires  a bit of leap in imagination, but I found myself naturally creating this narrative in my mind. In fact, it was hard NOT to feel immersed and invested in my troops, as the ingenious card-play seamlessly drove the narrative.

Chad Jensen has created a fascinating system here that allows for some truly memorable moments to be played out, within the timeless context of Axis and Allied conflict in the European theater of World War 2.

RATING: 9
Game not that complex (rules wise) for wargamers.  I found it very easy to learn the rules and dive into a first game - making a few look ups as needed.  But after that first play, I found that the system just flowed together rather nicely without having to look up results on charts and tables.  The cards do dictate what you can do and as a result, doing well takes practice as one needs to manage the cards in hand to coordinate your units to achieve the desired results.  And even then, success in combat is likely to be the result of attrition and slowly wearing down the opponent instead of a single devastating blow.  A very tactical game with a good abstraction of the chaos of war via the many events, snipers, etc.  I like the decisions and the need (and ability) to respond to the changing situation on the map.  Initial plans get revised and new tactics tried.  A scenario can be played in one sitting (ranging from 2 to 3 hours on average) but even if I lose, I have a wonderful narrative I can relate about what transpired during those tense hours.

RATING: 9
Great fun WW2 tactical game, playable usually in a couple hours, unusually clear well-written rules, great support in the forum by knowledgable players and occasionally Chad Jensen the designer.

It comes with 12 fine scenarios, but I have particularly high praise for the [b]random scenario generator[/b]. (And I wish more tactical games had good RSGs, or RSGs at all, for that matter. CC has spoiled me and raised my expectations in multiple ways!) I sometimes get the impression I'm the only CC player active in the forum who does not instantly auto-buy every CC expansion! I'm sure the expansions are great, but be aware that if you're not a completionist, or if you're just trying to save money or shelf space, then the random scenario generator [i]really does[/i] create an endless supply of great scenarios, so one doesn't [i]need[/i] to keep buying expansions to enjoy playing CC again and again!

[i]Comparisons with other WW2 tactical games:[/i]
Before CC I was playing Conflict of Heroes with a friend who owned it, and we both found we preferred CC. (But I am rather curious to try CoH again when its true solitaire module finally comes out, and CC's card-based core makes it notably less solo-friendly than non-card-based games for me, though I've tried solo CC a couple times.)

I've solo played some Panzer Grenadier, which is an interesting system, but notably slower and fiddlier than CC, so I doubt I'll ever convince a friend to play it with me, and I prefer CC.

I've recently started playing Band of Brothers, which has more simple elegant rules, but lacks the random event flavor and detail of CC, and like most tactical games, alas, lacks any kind of random scenario generator.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
At first I felt the card mechanic to be forced, and....well....mechanical. It in no way scratched my CDG itch like I thought it was going to. What the cards in fact do for you in the game is just limit your options on what you can do, not how much you can do. Normally you can activate 1 leader or a succession of leaders or x amount of spaces or whatever, and then move and/or shoot then melee/shock attack or whatever, then its your oppenents turn. In this game you can only move or shoot or assault if you have one of the corresponding cards. So if you don't have any "Fire" cards, then you're not shooting this turn, even if you can move all your units. Likewise with the "move" cards, if you ain't got any, you ain't movin', but you might be able to shoot. Sometimes, you just discard your whole hand cause you literally cannot do anything. That was sometimes frustrating, but at the same time it takes out alot of the AP and numbers crunching out of the game, and reduces the ruleset to something almost anybody could understand. I think what the cards take away as far as simulation and the natural ebb and flow of combat games really adds in playability and streamlinedness. Although I can see it getting kind of old if played too much.

***Update***
Bumped up to a 8.5 with further play. Card Mechanic is genius and takes alot of the "what should I do now" out of the game, as you only have a few options. The random events and such make it great fun to. For example, in my latest game I sniped one of my own units and my opponents sent Artillery down on himself. Game is not long enough for this to encite rage, as PoG or something would have it doing.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
A real surprise. I bought this as a gift for my SO, who wanted a light wargame to play. I personally didn't expect to like it much - but I really did. 

I'm now almost always up for a game. I like the light-but-still present tactics, the effective portreyal of the limited options in battle, the story each game tells... Quite frankly, this is one of the best wargames I've ever played.

RATING: 7
It's got your same basic ASL unit size and scenarios, but it's driven by a card mechanic that throws in a large number of random events and severely limits your control over your forces. While this has the effect of throwing some spice into the gameplay, it can also be odd at times to spend your turns fishing through your deck while enemy troops take a leisurely stroll right by your position. To me this cries out for some sort of reduced intensity fire option if you don't have a card, but that's not what they're going for in Combat Commander. If you want more control and realism then I'd suggest ASL - there's some good starter packs to get you going. If you want a smooth flowing card driven system that limits your options each turn, but injects some battlefield chaos and plot twists into the mix then CC:E is a good choice. Hard to get started, but once you've made the effort to learn the system it's a good little game.

RATING: 7
After reading [i][url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/22825]Tide of Iron[/url][/i]'s rules, it seems like these games are very similar to each other.  Both are simulations of World War II tactical combat and, in general, the players act in both as commanding leaders of a platoon of infantry and of some other company attachments, like vehicles and artillery.  Of course, the differences are also big between them, being the main one that [i][url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/21050]Combat Commander: Europe[/url][/i] is a card driven game with easier rules at first sight.  Nevertheless, both are very nice games that scratch real good the World War II wargamey itch.  Speaking of other things, [i]Combat Commander: Europe[/i] looks in a way like an upgraded and fortified [i][url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/10630]Memoir '44[/url][/i], which makes it a nice option for newbies or casual players, and, at the same time, its simplicity doesn't take away the depth and realism, which makes it a good pick also for gamers.  My rating definitively is going to get higher as I play the game more often and start to see the different strategies and combinations.

RATING: 4
I played this a fair amount and it never really clicked with me.  I had several issues with it.  First, I did not like the lack of control.  I often found myself frustrated by not being able to do basic things like move or fire because I did not have the right cards.  I don't mind that in shorter, lighter games like Memoir, but there is too much chaos for a 3 hour medium weight wargame.  At least with a true CDG like Washington's War you can always use a card for operations if needed.  Not true here.  A second problem is that the victory conditions seemed too gamey to me.  For example, you could score points by moving your men off the edge of the board.  They would later reappear on a different edge.  That just seems weird.  A third problem was game length.  Our games lasted 3 hours +, which is too long for a game as random as this.  A fourth problem was that it seemed too hard to kill enemy units.  It was hard enough to hit an enemy, which would normally just break them.  And then after you broke them, they would usually rally before you could score another hit.  From a game perspective just slowed the game and gave me the feeling of being in a knife fight using butter knives.  This game is one of my bigger disappointments.  I really wanted to like this game and invested a fair amount of time in it.  

UPDATE April 2013:  Tried this again and still disappointed.  This game just does not do anything for me.  I don't know what others see in it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Combat Commander really is Up Front crossed with Squad Leader.  Evolutionary, not revolutionary... but very highly evolved for that.  You lose the perfect control of SL but gain solid topography and visual nuance over UF.  Everything is very procedural, and no question was left unresolved by a quick reference to the rules, which are well-indexed.  I question the value of the Suppressed status vs. its rules overhead, and the way broken Weapons are fixed/eliminated seems a little wonky, but overall the seamless integration of chaos and tactics works very well.  The rules are tight, and Chad seems to have thought of everything.  I've played some randomly generated scenarios lately, and they are at least as interesting and cool as the "fixed" ones.  90 minutes is optimistic - 3 hours to learn the first scenario, and I have a hard time seeing play time get below 2 hours.  Easier to learn than Up Front, but not by much - not even remotely approaching Euro territory.

UPDATE: EARLY 2011
Having played a lot of ASLSK lately, and thereby seeing where a lot of bits of CC came from, I am less enamored of the way they are used here.  CC is still an interesting game, but it looks more Frankensteinish to me now.  The RSG alone is worth the price of admission.

UPDATE: LATE 2011
As I dig deeper into ASL, I have less patience for the oddness of CC.

RATING: 6
Up Front with maps and smoothed out rules (and that's about the highest compliment I can bestow on a game).  There is some SL in here as well, but the game plays much more like Up Front, and it has virtually none of the rulebook rummaging that goes along with SL or especially ASL.  In just about every case, the designer has opted for simplicity over minutia, and yet the overall experience feels very authentic.  I was a little worried about replayability when I saw the small map sizes, but every game has felt different and fresh to this point, and the scenarios are widely varied in terms of terrain and force types.  (I still haven't found one that has grabbed me like the old Guard's Counterattack, though.)

Update- I have always enjoyed playing this one, but I've soured somewhat on the way that chaos is modeled (it's not that I don't like chaos, but that I don't think the model here works properly).  It's fun to play, but there are better squad level games out there.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Up Front meets Squad Leader. Playable in 1.5 hours? What more can I say? MMP has just lost many potential sales of an upfront reprint to his wee game.

How does it work? Players pick a scenario, set up their units and have a hand of cards to drive the game. The cards are fairly similar across the board and act to activate units to move, rout, recover, advance and request arty. There are also bonus actions that can supplement regular play as well as a die randomizer (2D6 x 72 rolls).

So far so good. However, while the game mechanics work fine, it seems to degenerate down to a level of burning cards to get the right one/s for the situation. Historical accuracy is sacrificed for playability and this often results in strange results. (EG: Walking wounded can end up 'teleporting' a lost unit onto the board, one might get a high scoring secret object next to where they setup units and score easy victory points).

The rules are brief but leave room for much ambiguity (EG: What happens when a moving group takes op fire and breaks?) and games seem to take longer than advertised. I think that trying to mesh SL and Upfront is a good idea in principle but CC:E ends up being too ambitious and somewhat of a hit and miss.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2.45
Boy, did I ever have a bad experience with this one!  First of all I'm not a big fan of most World War II games or a lot of tactical warfare games, although I've always been a fan of the game "Ambush!" and tend to think "Band of Brothers" is probably the best thing ever filmed for television.  I think my Russians spent 4 1/2 hours shooting two heavy machine guns.  Maybe it wasn't that long, but it felt much longer than that.  They didn't scratch a damn thing!  So I ask my instructor, if I need to move the machine guns up.  No, apparently there's no bonus for being closer.  My Russians can only kill Germans bare-handed.  That doesn’t feel like World War II to me!  The game definitely needs a pair of dice.  The "cards used as dice" feature is just silly.  There was no artillery in my scenario, so half the cards were useless.  The artillery cards could not be omitted since that would violate the 36-card multiple needed to replicate all the rolls for a pair of 6-sided dice.  We must have had 20 sniper cards that were activated during my game.  They seemed to be everywhere.  That made no sense.  Most of the game didn't make any sense to me.  My opponent and I spent most of the game discarding our hands and waiting to acquire fire and movement cards.  This game seems to take some of the worst aspects of the Command and Colors-type games and makes them worse.  If I remember correctly, I believe you can use the cards in most of the Command and Colors-type games to either move and/or fire.  The problem with this game is that the move and fire cards are split, making your troops much more difficult to command.  If you're out of fire cards, you can't even shoot a couple of troops running across an open field.  This type of card system might be okay for a game like "Bang," but it seems quite inappropriate for a two-player game with a more serious theme such as this one.  At one point I attempted to place two counters in the same hex, probably not the best tactics, but this is not allowed because all soldiers in this game are apparently colossal fast bastards sort of like Mr. Creosote in Monty Python's The Meaning of Life.  If they can't move or shoot properly, perhaps they could explode on the enemy by attempting to digest a solitary thin wafer.  

UPDATE: The more I think about it I think a lot of the game's problems can be solved by replaced the card system's use of dice with a pair of actual dice.  This would allow players to toss out cards inappropriate to a scenario which would speed up the game.  I also think that a player should have a chance to move or fire without having an actual move or fire card.  I would say that any player should always have about a 83% (5/6) chance to fire and a 50% chance to move at ANY time.  I can understand a soldier's fear to move into a dangerous area, but the inability to fire a weapon seems rather ridiculous unless the soldiers are really pinned down.  The sniper cards also seem to be a bit too much in many ways.

UPDATE #2:  Another thing against the game is that the card "dice roll distribution" is fixed, meaning that only one 6-6 or 1-1 will appear for each set of 36 cards, killing off much of the randomness.

RATING: 3
Although this game is maybe the most elaborate and innovative wargame in recent years, it has issues.

The game is frustrating because sometimes (or when you're unlucky; all the time) your squads can neither shoot nor fire. Some people argue that this is realistic, - I don't have any combat experience to dis-/prove this point, but from my understanding an elite unit accompanied by a competent leader should be able to move when there's no enemy unit in sight. The same goes for a MG in a prepared defensive position which should be able to fire at advancing units when manned by an attentive crew and when the gun is neither jammed, unloaded nor otherwise restricted in its functionality.

Defensive positions are almost impenetrable. As opposed to other games, satchel charges, panzerfaust rockets and even artillery strikes leave houses or pillboxes unscratched. Preparing units for close combat against defenders in these fortifications is a pain in the arse, because you need a good set of "Advance", "Fire" and "Movement" cards, which is very hard to build. And don't get me started on melee combat.

Furthermore you only lose units through bad luck. You can have a unit fired at by an enemy flame-thrower and this is no problem when you have a "Recover"-card at hand.

The system is also very gamey. Firing at units without any chance of success, just to use up cards and "advance in time", is a plausible tactic, as is blatantly ignoring one's objective to move units past the enemy.

The many possible random events spice up the game, but some are just illogical in some situations.

The game is a really neat design, but it fails to simulate combat in WWII, when compared to [gameID=1035] for example. Compared to this old shoe, it's also only slightly more fun. Another war might really fit better, I thought about WWI trench warfare, but this in not relevant to this comment.

This rating is very subjective and displays the disappointment I had missing this game's supposed greatness.

I might want to try [gameID=28181] though, if it contains some rule changes or a different composition of the cards.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Sikerült kipróbálni az egyik top wargame-t, amiben kimondottan közelről látjuk az eseményeket. Katonákat irányítunk, akik fegyvereket is magukkal vihetnek egy pár négyzetkilométeres területen. Változatos térképeket kapunk a játékhoz, amin bőven van lehetőség taktikázni. Fontosabb célpontok szerepelnek rajta, illetve a győzelmi pont szerzéshez egységeink kilépése a térképről is lehetőség az ellenfél oldalán. Ezen túl vannak nyitott plusz célok és lehetnek rejtettek is. A játék kártyavezérelt. Egy-egy kártyán három fő dolog szerepel. Vagy parancsként, vagy akcióként kerülhet kijátszásra, illetve bizonyos módon eseményként is. Nekem kicsit az tetszett kevésbé, hogy ezek nem túl változatosak, bár ez első játékra talán merész kijelentés, de valahogy a TS teljesen különböző kártyáihoz szokva volt más. Az viszont zseniális megoldás, hogy a kockadobás előre generált a kártyákon. A teljes paklinkban két d6 dobás összes lehetséges variációja fordul elő és így nincs szükség tényleges dobásra és a szerencse is kisebb, mert ha rossz dobások jönnek is, idővel akkor jók maradnak. A játék elsőre is megérhető, de azért nehezebb, mint egy Conflict of Heroes és jelenleg én még azt érzem nem ezzel a gémmel tölteném el az időm. Ettől függetlenül nem véletlen a sikere. Egy jó játékról van szó, ami azért bárkivel nem játszható és inkább idősebbeknek való, sőt talán nyugdíjas korra részemről.
