RATING: 9.2
The game is very fluid and is driven by cards. Even though you can see the enemy all over the board you still have to have the right orders in hand to execute your strategy. There are many random events that can effect units not directly involved in combat. So if you got a big fat elite squad just sitting on top of and objective and dug in, event triggers can also effect them as well. I once had six squads of Russian infantry poised to attack a few Germans, but could not get my orders to activate them, meanwhile the Germans could execute and slowly picked apart the command paralyzed Russian units in their tracks. The game is not complicated and plays decent solitaire. There are no tanks or planes but can be abstractly represented by certain random events. The big bonus is the random scenario generator, giving you the ability to create virtually endless battles. Games last from 1-2 hours but can finish quicker if you eliminate enough enemy units to cause a surrender result. This game plays beyond the borders of the map, I have never experienced that in any wargame before. I am very pleased with this game and can't say enough good things about it.

RATING: 9
As has been said before, this game does have similarities with both ASL and Up Front.  It's very streamlined though and easier (by far) to learn than those other two games so it makes an excellent introduction to small unit tactical games.

The artwork on the maps and counters is very pretty and the cards are stylish as well as chock full of data.  These cards are the heart and soul of the game.  They are used for orders, actions, random events, combat results etc. etc.  It is a very clean and playable system.

The criticism most often made of Combat Commander is that the system is too 'random'.  I can't actually deny that - so if you don't like games where you have less than total control over things don't buy this one!  I quite like the chaos and randomness generated by the system but can easily see how it would piss some people off.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
This is a very cool game, although I think it's likely still too much of a wargame for me. I love the card-driven nature of it, where the cards are used to:
- issue orders on your turn (e.g., move, fire, advance one step with no op-fire possible, call in an artillary strike, recover -- flip a broken unit back to full strength, rout the other player -- force them to make checks on their broken units)
- take actions on either player's turn (e.g., ambush them in melee, opportunity fire, modify combat strength)
- make dice rolls (every card has 2d6 at the bottom)
- trigger events (trigger words on die rolls)
- enact events (e.g., sniper fires at a hex, weapons break, weather effects)
- choose a random hex (hexes are coded; each card shows one such code)
- advance game rounds (each time a player depletes their deck, advance one step on the time track; defender also gains 1VP and other stuff may also trigger)

But I don't like:
- setup: wow, there are a lot of chits to dig through to set up for your scenario!
- charts: there are charts and charts for stuff like reinforcements, terrain modifier summaries, etc. I just don't want to have to deal with that stuff! Of course, after playing a lot of this likely becomes unnecessary.
- dealing with little chits during game play. I like how 1941 has big chunky tiles

On the other hand, game play is pretty cool. I generally don't have a feel for combat tactics, and stuggle to figure out what I should be doing at any given moment. But card-driven games help you in that respect: you can only do what the cards allow you to do! Maybe I will try this on Vassal some time ...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Combat Commander has been created with the desire to make a sharp contrast with other tactical games that give too much control to some sort of übercommander to launch a perfectly cohesive assault with multiple squads. Here, your forces will behave, on the contrary, like a student project's robot under heavy rain. You might be able to move an "arm", then maybe a "leg", but you may not be able to move that other "arm" for turns (if ever), because of serious command and movement limitations. Maybe you  want to move thoses forces to flank here, while attacking there, but then, how many turns will this take if you don't have 3 "Move" cards in hand already (and you won't) ? 4, 5... 6 ? How long can you wait for that elusive "Move" card ? Will your planned tactic even be possible later ? 

You could send those squads through an open field far away from enemy forces, then you don't a move card and they may stay there for turns until this enemy MG is right there to shoot at you - and you still won't be able to budge. I once had a unit adjacent to an objective after a movement, just waiting to get inside while staying covered behind it in the meantime. But I had to use a "Move" card elsewhere and it just sat there waiting until enemies came from 9 hexes away to take the objective in 3 turns and I was unable to move in before they did. What were my units doing there all that time ? Smoking in front of the farm ? Talking to the farmer's daughter ? You can go wild trying to make up explnanations, but at which do you go from a system that favors some form of chaos to pure non-sense ? I just wish the movement system was not dependant upon the fate decks...

 There's a few other things I have problems with here: squads are closely tied to leaders, which are so important that the loss of one might sometimes be so tragic for your moving capacity, you'd better capitulate. Opporunity fire that depends on cards (I was able to move 5 squads in front of an MG in open terrain because I could see, from one of them moving, that my opponent was out of "fire" cards). There's also the powerful "recover" cards that can annul LONG-prepared assaults in a blink, units that score VP coming out of the board before they come right back on the other side; some strange, illogical events, but those are all minor things. My only real complaint is the slow, tedious task of moving largely uncoordinated units on the map, having to stop to wait for "Move" cards that just don't want to show up. Taking turns and turns to just discard all of those "artillery denied" or "command confusion" cards just kills the pace of the game.

I don't want to be too severe here, because I believe that there is a lot to like also. Combat is fast and efficient, graphics on the units are great, and many ideas are well implemented, like the "actions" and some of the events. I also like the system concerning the objective chits, the way time passes, the surrender rule, etc. But if you like your tactical games to be fast, furious, dynamic and in-your-face, you might want to look elsewhere.

RATING: 9
A quite steep learning curve. An unexpected depth among the apparent chaos of the Fate cards, with a narrative unfolding before your own eyes.

[Nov-2013]: my rating upgraded to 8 after 4 games with a good and patient mentor. The interest lies in the difficulty to learn how to balance the command energy needed to progress into the scenario. Beautiful design, high replayability. Warning: an experienced player is a big plus to learn the game and flatten the learning curve. Two beginners in the tactical wargame field will struggle before starting to appreciate the game - the first few games can be very frustrating.
Reading the developer's notes by John Foley published in C3i magazine (available online as PDF) is recommended to every CC:E player, it helps in understanding the flow and the management of the hand cards.

[Update Aug-2014] Rating gone up to 9, after a furious summer seeing several scenarios from the base game and the CC:Med expansion being played - I have been hooked for good! These games were entertaining and engaging, and gave me a lot of fun in terms of narrative. Once the basics have been mastered, the discovery of the different tactical layersof this design is very pleasant. I even had a couple of solo runs of CC:E - the game is working amazingly well.
My main recommandation is to learn the game with an experienced player to avoid all the pitfalls of the tactical field.

RATING: 10
I'm not going to bother reviewing the gameplay as their are countless great reviews of this game. Suffice it to say I agree with most of them. I will comment that this is at the very top of my complexity acceptability and I'm struggling to keep all the rules straight in my head, not to speak of the nested action sequences which can be tricky to track. It's slowly coming together but I look forward to the day when I can stop constantly referencing the rulebook and player aids.
I should also comment that I don't really care for WW2. I don't remember how many people are in a team or a squad though the rulebook did say, I don't know or care what a volksgrenadier is or if a German pioneer is the same as the American engineers (those numbers sure looked the same to me!) or how a guards rifle unit differs from a regular rifle unit. But the game just manages to capture the imagination so completely that I forget all those things and just focus on keeping my little guys alive in the swirl of battlefield mayhem. It is fantastic.

Scenarios
1.FL was not a very interesting scenario but was simple enough to learn on, at least using the basic strategy of grabbing and holding the lake house (a drawn chit raised the VP value of this by 10). That raised the stakes as the Germans did their best to hold on but at the end couldn't keep the overwhelming firepower from the forest at bay. The Russians who had unbelievably lucky draws throughout the game advanced and the Germans were sent packing. They had been holding out for one more time trigger for quite a while but it just never came for them. 
2.Wasn't a fan of Hedgerows and Hand-grenades.
3.Bonfire of the NKVD was good fun. The Russians fighting retreat didn't go quite to plan and they had to surrender well before the end.
4.Closed for Renovations was suitably stabby and ended quickly as the smoke cup seemingly pulled out only values of 5 and up across the board, well before the reinforcements arrived.
5.Cold Front - the dash across the river played out well with a tight win by the Germans on VP primarily due to some timely reinforcements.
7.Poor Germans were obliterated by some very lucky Russians who managed to grab a hero and a .50cal and then stuck them with the pointy end in close combat all across the board. Ended by Surrender with the Russians on 26VP!
8.Very poor setup by the Russians allowed the Germans, by a couple of lucky events to break and rout 3 russian units off the board, and then do a dash through the brush whilst popping off smoke to get off the board. The rest of the Germans managed to spread the russian forces but were playing more for time and locations (drawn objective) than trying to escape. Easy German victory.

RATING: N/A
Seems an interesting take on wargaming. Card-driven decision making, random events thrown in the mix, secret objectives. 

Not for me though. Guess I'm more fan of 'light-hearted' wargaming such as Battlelore, Memoir '44, Heroes of Normandie. The 'traditional' crap components and traditionals fallbacks of wargaming (paper map with bland art, carton chits for units, spreadsheets lined up besides the map, fiddly tracks with critical markers, tons of little rules and exceptions to those rules) still being there sure don't help either.

Guess I have to grow more into wargaming. Might be a great game if you are really into 'heavy' wargaming, don't mind (or even are accustomed to) the components and you dig all those 'reality' rules, but not for me (yet).

That all being said, would like to play against someone that can really demo this game well to see if I can grow into it. As there seems a ton of variety in it so pretty sure this game can be absolutely awesome if you get past the learning curve, bland art and crappy components (compared to 'regular' boardgames, not other wargames).

RATING: 9
A few of my pals told me how great this game was.  How much narrative, how much tension, how much fun the chaos was. 

I was skeptical.  

I don't really get war games most of the time.  As much as I hate to admit it, I do place a decent amount of weight into how a game looks, feels, presents itself.  It is just too hard to get my wife (or most other gaming buddies) into war games.  

This game is far superior to most of the other games I own--it is that simple.  It is actually in another class to be fair.  It does not try to present anything with elegance, classy mechanics, or wacko stuffy themes.  Nope.  It just does what it does, and it does it awfully well.

It is a war game.  Therefore it is different from many other popular games on this site.  There is no farming, no kings to impress, no engines to build efficiently, there is nothing but war and chaos and an ingenious use of cards to throw it all up in the air.  

And it sticks.

RATING: 4
What a disappointment!  The game simply swings so far toward "anarchy" on the 'God/control to Chaos' spectrum that it robs players of any coherent course of action.  No planning, no ability to rely on the most basic of actions.  The game should have been 'card assisted' instead of 'card driven.'  Let me give you one example;

In most tacticals, running men across an open field in enemy LOS is one of highlights of the game, where the chance of enemy fire killing your men is at its greatest, but something that an attacker must do in order to advance.  In CC, if the opponent has no fire card, you stroll across without a shot.  Period.  Its not just unrealistic, but boring, robbing you of that adrenaline rush.

I was frankly astonished that even the most basic of squad actions required a card to execute.  The cards should have been soley for unit activation and random event/clock advancement, with subsequent unit behavior following tactical wargame standards.

However, I do credit the designers with integrating random events, the clock, and other features into the card deck.  its those elemnts that can bog down an ASL scenario, and the mechanic works to include those things while maintaining the game flow.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3.5
First Impression:
This game offers company level battles in great detail. Everything is included (except vehicles), from air support/artillery down to weapon jam and the occassional soldier tripping. All in just 2-3 hours per battle. The narrative after each battle is quite simply a small novel...

BUT

As a game, it plays itself. Really. You'll try to form some plans, but the game is so chaotic that they'll rarely work. Something quite unexpected will give you or the enemy the edge. Sometimes it will be the interperetation of some random combat element (that randomly appears out of nowhere) or the gamey fact that you simply didn't draw the correct orders/actions first. Which... sucks. The game is supposed to tone down luck by having dice results printed on cards, but honestly that's a laugh for anyone with basic understanding of statistics. And as if a bad combination of hand-management and random chaos wasn't enough to ruin this wargame, combat is ridiculusly unbalanced favoring melee! I thought this was ww2, not the middle ages!


To sum it up, this game keeps you drooling, but never really delivers.

RATING: 7
I've been toying with the idea of wargames lately and was happy to have the opportunity to give Combat Commander a shot. I'd only played a few before, and generally the simpler and perhaps somewhat atypical ones, such as Hammer of the Scots, Twilight Struggle, and Bonaparte at Marengo. I've found that I don't really love wargames. I don't think they're exactly for me. I like simplicity in my games and streamlined rules without many exceptions or caveats, which doesn't seem to gel with the wargame mentality. I also found this out when I started a thread on BGG seeking wargame recommendations and people asked what time period I was interested in, and I said I didn't care at all. Apparently people playing wargames are often interested in the background and time period, which I am not. That all being said, I do enjoy the wargames that I own mentioned above (i.e., HotS, TS, BaM), and was happy to have the chance to play Combat Commander. I liked how the cards were so versatile, including being used for die rolls, which I thought was clever. I did feel it was odd in my one game how we had this big huge map with a bunch of units, but the fighting got bogged down in one contested area, then again maybe it was just a quirk of that match or maybe it really was a key, decisive area.

RATING: 10
This game, one that I was able to play with Chad some years ago, the same day that he had planned to meet with the Gentlemen of GMT to see if they might "take it on," finally came in the mail, after a relatively short stay on the P-500.  The game plays Much Faster when playing with Chad, as rules were never in need of checking, yet, finding the rules necessary to play the game properly were sensationally easy to locate the exact rule desired.  This is a huge plus, and benefit for those of us who have been frustrated by the organization of rulebooks having to do with historical simulations.  This, hearkens a renewal to the days of Avalon Hill, that, in my estimation, and humble opinion, were the epitome of ease in introduction of rules, concepts, and follow-up, including a Designer's Page, which CC has. 

Simply the most fun a guy can have with cardboard counters!

RATING: 6
This game is my first foray into wargaming proper, and I'm not 100% certain that it's my thing.  It's an interesting system, and it seems straightforward enough to learn and play.  The game does have a lot of exceptions and fiddly details to remember to apply at the right times, but after about halfway through the game, they make a lot of sense.  The card-driven mechanism is also interesting, and clever in the way that the cards can do four different things, but it is possible to be hosed by an unlucky draw, which can be fairly frustrating.  I can see how this system would be a great one for wargamers, but I'm not sure if it's for me.  I would certainly play it again.

1 play: 2 players (Fat Lipki)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is in my opinion the best squad level wargame ever created. Others are more detailed, but nothing tells a story quite like Combat Commander. Every scenario feels meticulously researched, yet the game engine ensures that every scenario feels alive...even when you play it on more than one occasion. A game of this is like an interactive viewing of Saving Private Ryan. Your best guys will die by random snipers, isolated units will rise to greatness under the spontaneous generation of a new leader, when one of your privates take control of a hairy situation and rallies his unit. It's an incredible experience, every time I play.

The basic system is reminiscent of the Command and Colors games from Richard Berg, but there are so many more glorious details. Random events, heroic actions, and command paralysis, all will affect every battle. On top of all this, if you ever inexplicably get tired of all the historical scenarios, there is a random scenario generator and it's utterly brilliant.

Honestly, I believe I could sell every game in my collection and as long as I could hold on to this, I'd be able to live with it. It's that good.

RATING: 8
Yet another long time player of ASL & UF cracks open CC:E. 1. bought the Para & CC:M modules
2. clipped the counters!

Only an 8 instead of a 9 due to:
1. lack of single atack KIA (a single leader running in the street can be shot at point blank by an entire platoon, all their MGs and a flurry of grenades, and still just suffer nothing worse than a 'broken' result.
2. no multi-level buildings.
3. apparent LOS conflict between T88.3.1 & T88.4.1 which suggests that hills are both taller and shorter than buildings/woods because you can see over them, but are not tall enough to cast a blind zone?

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Two player Axis vs. Allies scenario based WWII strategy war game. Card driven squad level tactics that use 12 different paper terrain maps. This game has a bunch of new(or not seen often) and clever mechanics for me that could(should) be used in other genres; tug-o-war style victory point track, casualty track triggers game end, random scenario generator with random open/secret objectives, love the element of when to use the iniative marker, time track triggers scenario "sudden death". Each side plays from a nationality fate deck. The cards serve multi-use for dice roll amounts which can trigger events. I love how rounds of time are not predictable and advance from events or dice rolls. As a wargame newb, I love the many detailed action choices and how their play conditions interact with the units and terrain. Game includes inset to organize unit tokens. Leader units can influence adjacent units. I love how broken troops can become more dangerous despite being weaker. Eek does a great job breaking this game down.

RATING: 10
Adequately detailed WWII tactical combat with a healthy dose of chaotic fun!  Good narrative feel, good game.

Update (10/19/2009):  This is the WWII tactical game for me.  You don't have total control, but you have enough control.  The rules are easier than the length of the rulebook suggests because the rulebook describes every card in detail, but the text on the cards is adequate for actual play.  I like managing the chaos that the game throws at you.  Games are tense and frustrating in a good way.  There's a good variety of units and weapons.  The included scenarios are varied and fun.  I'm looking forward to giving the (reputedly) excellent random scenario generator a try.  The rule book is amazingly clear and well-organized.  There is excellent support for the game both here and on CSW.  You can have a lifetime of fun with just the CC:E set, but there are also plenty of expansions coming out at regular intervals.  This will likely become a 10 for me after not too much longer.

Update 8/9/11:  I've gotten a few RSG games in.  It's great!  Making your way through the RSG is a mini-game before the main event that has a real influence on the "actual" game play.

RATING: 5
Not my cup of tea. Making tactical plans is mostly useless, because gameplay consists of reacting to the cards you happen to draw and all the randomness of every subsystem. From a tactical point of view, everything that happens is very counterintuitive. Both defensive and offensive fire is laughably ineffective compared to a melee rush. The card driven engine is a remarkably bad choice for this type of game.

RATING: 9
Only played the walk through and solo so far and I was surprisingly and worryingly underwhelmed. I think I've listened to too much hype and enthusiastic hyperbole.

Game play .. well I was expecting the randomness so that's fine. What I didn't expect was the lack of feel of real world tactics. I get much more of that from CoH. Maybe experience will prove me wrong there.

Components wise .. well many people told me it was a visual treat and for the most part everything works well together. I don't appreciate having to use a scalpel to get the chits from the board though; they rip too easily without it. And I hate paper maps. Sorry but I do although I can see how the cost and weight would make heavy ones impractical considering the number of them.

The chits that are used on the display track are too small and insubstantial, fiddly and get easily lost and overlooked. As are hex changing chits such as fire, which affects a whole hex and makes it impassable yet is just a little square of card that isn't immediately obvious. I've not come across a reason why that isn't hex sized and shaped other than financial.

While the 1 counter tray is a generous addition its useless as it doesn't hold all the chits in any useful organised manner. You need 3. 

edit: 8 April 2010. After another couple of play throughs I'm still totally underwhelmed. I can see why some people like the story side of it as you never do have any idea of what'll happen next. The randomness and unpredictability feel all consuming, that the enemy is the game itself rather than my opponent. I'm going to try one of the bigger Stalingrad scenarios to see if real world tactics have any say in it at all.

So far its a 5 because at least the components have a fairly consistent and interesting visual style if lower quality than I'd expect for the price.

edit: 9th May .. after a few more plays I think I'm getting it. It requires the same mind set as Mem 44 (despite the many objections people seem to have to this comparison). I hate the idea of just thinking of it as a card management game because I find breaking a game down to simple mechanics turns them into a series clinical abstract concepts and takes the fun and immersion out of them. But if I can imagine it as a leader desperately trying to rally his men, communicate under difficult circumstances, react to an ever changing environment while still meeting his objectives .. then I can rationalise it and feel the game rather than just executing it. I'm upping it to a 7.

edit: 16/12/2010 many solo and a number of head to head plays and its a great game. Questions of randomness don't even occur to now. Its just the chaos of battle and events outside your control and everything adds to the story and oozes theme. While it would be nice to have tanks, most WW2 engagements didn't involve them so its not a huge hardship and does keep it simple(r). I'm going to ramp this upto a 9.

Update: 02/2011 .. played almost all of the CC:E Scenarios F2F and it just keeps getting better.

RATING: 10
This game has provided for some of the most intense moments in my gaming experiences.  The card mechanic is just so great!  Where some may complain about the need to discard and, thus, lose your turn, my opponent and I have consistently found this to *increase* the tension and excitement in the game rather than diminish those aspects.
